=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An early, and light dice-rolling worker-placement game in the same genre as To Court a King, Alea Iacta Est, Troyes, Alien Frontiers, Castles of Burgundy, Pirates of Nassau, and La Venise Du Nord. Kingsburg predates all except To Court a King, and is a pretty succinct, accessible design with great artwork. There's nothing particularly deep here - simple engine building really, with a heavy dose of luck and blocking involved - but it is fun, and a lot easier to explain and pick up than the brilliant, heavy, Troyes.

6+ plays / 4,5 players

DISLIKE: Low replayability. Play the game enough and the winning strategies become common and repetitive, but there is enough there for quite a few plays. Bad dice rolls can be frustrating.

OVERALL: Even with the expansion I feel the boards need to differentiate more to force new strategies. Fun game, wish it played just a tad quicker.

RATING: 8.5
A fun, slightly more weightier than normal gateway game - certainly as regards the amount of rules.  I like the die rolling mechanic which limits rather than decides the game. I like the invading hordes at the end of each year, which seems like a Stephan Feld-type idea. I would have liked them to have slightly more teeth, though. It's also got elements that will remind you of Caylus and several other worker placement games.  Perhaps it's slighlty too long for a filler, which seems a shame as it may be even better if it was quicker - but perhaps one could limit time for each decision with a chess clock - maybe not on the first game as there's a lot to learn, but after that.  In this respect and a few others, it reminded me a little of Tribune.

The expansion looks very worthwhile.

RATING: 7
*Rating based on one play.

This game makes pretty good use of dice as "workers" in a worker-placement style game.  It feels like a better game than Alien Frontiers, which uses a similar mechanic.  In Alien Frontiers, you literally cannot plan out your turn before play passes back to you because you have no idea what you will roll.  In this game, you can plan out your moves ahead of time (at least, as much as you can in any worker placement game).  I would also say this game reminds me of Castles of Burgundy, though it's been so long since I've played Burgundy that I can't say which I would prefer of the two.

+Straightforward gameplay that doesn't overstay its welcome.
+Solid component quality and artwork.
+Good level of strategic and tactical play.
+Multiple paths to victory.

-Rolling low is generally worse than rolling high. I prefer dice games where roll outcomes aren't necessarily better or worse, just different.
-Theme is fairly pasted on and not particularly engaging.

RATING: 8.35
A light and enjoyable resource-collection and building game, with a light fantasy theme.  

Personally, I think this is a better game with the expansion, which at least allows for the pursuit of strategies that have a better chance of defeating the embassy strategy (see my comments to the expansion below).

Very tight game in terms of victory points, which means over-investment in defensive buildings may not be a winning strategy.  The risk-reward trade-off of neglecting defense and going for building strategies which yield more victory points is a nice touch.  I've had a couple of unfortunate run-ins with barbarians in the late game which caused me to lose the game because I had neglected a bit on defense.  

New printing available with worse art, but which comes with all the expansion modules out of the box.

RATING: 6
Good dice game.  The mechanisms (dice placement, resource gathering, building construction) combine to make a good dice-based game.  Two criticisms.  One, low dice rolls will  have a significant effect on outcome/VPs.  Yes, this often arises with dice-based games.  However, I feel it is particularly true with Kingsburg as there is little-to-no catch up mechanism.  Two, the building construction does not make for many interesting choices.  I get the feeling that there is a certain building progression at work.  Plus, there's a good chance that of the 10-15 buildings everyone constructs, a good number of them will be common across the board.  There is little differentiation.

A fun game?  Certainly!  Interesting choices?  Occasionally.  The building angle?  Well done but no scarcity or direct player interaction via or involving buildings.  Player interaction is *only* via dice placement.

I would definitely play this one again, but I doubt I'll pick it up (unless it's on sale and marked way down, of course).

ADDENDUM (01 July 2013):  Friend of mine gave me this and the expansion.  Expansion fixes the biggest problems and provides a TON of replay value.  The expansion makes this a good game.  My rating of the base game reflects just the base game.

RATING: 5.5
[With To Forge a Realm]

The base game's a little too simple, but overall this is the most interesting I've found "worker placement" (Dominant Species is a wargame that happens to use WP of a kind).

However, there is a problem: the pure base game makes military too luckish, and the chance of losing a battle is high even with prep, unless you give up too much effort to military (which means a lucky opponent can beat you just by taking a risk).  The expansion ramps up the power level of the player (via governor powers and the planning ability of the military tokens) to the point you are unlikely to lose, however.  Is there a sweet spot?  If not, the game ends up not being very enjoyable.

Also, even with the expansion the buildings are a little too much "same old same old."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great game, my only wish was that it were shorter.  This is not at all a dicefest, its a drafting mechanic where those who roll less have less drafting power but get to go first, with the result that most rolls in the 8-13 are approximately even in value.  Only very very low rolls or very high rolls are weaker/stronger than average, and even then, not by a whole lot usually.  Tactics/die placement and building choice tends to matter more than the size of your die rolls.  There is some luck but not more than your average middleweight game, imo.

I like playing with the monster-die variant to make mosnters more difficult (because they seem extremely easy with the base rules).  Roll two diece, subtract the lower from the higher and thats how many soldiers you all get.  (Alternately, giving people chips with numbers 0,1,1,2,3,4 and they get to place one per round for how much they personally get, with the extra one being points at game end).

RATING: 8
I think that Kingsburg is a fantastic worker placement game that in my mind is better than Puerto Rico and Caylus. The reason it's so good is that you simply place your dice on what you want and then collect it to purchase a building on your own player mat. It's easy to become familiar with the different buildings as they are always in front of you, and it's a direct means of collecting abilities and victory points. For many worker placement games, there's a bunch of extra steps involved (building plantation, shipping goods, etc.) that can be fiddly and cumbersome. Kingsburg is also very suitable for new gamers as a gateway game as it introduces the worker placement genre in an easy going, accessible way. 

The only negative that I see is Kingburg might turn into San Juan, where the quickest path to victory is building certain buildings in relative order (such as the Inn row where you eventually get an extra dice) and then the Crane (build higher level buildings for one less gold). We shall see - we've only played one vanilla game so far. We did play again with the expansion pack, which I felt diminished the elegant simplicity of the game.

BGG review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/328797/kingsburg-a-detailed-review

RATING: 8.8
A very fun Eurogame, but it has DICE!  Very strange for a Euro, but very fun and unique way to choose your action for the turn.  I think this game will have a decent amount of replayability.  The only complaint I have is that the combat at the end of each year is very VERY luck based.  You normally have between 2-6 defense on your own, and you all gain 1d6 for defending.  This means that half of your position in combat is complete luck, which makes the combat feel out of place in this otherwise elegant game.

RATING: 6
2-5(4)

Okay, so it's reasonably fun. Not all that fiddly, and gets pretty intuitive. The first few plays the intuition is to grab the most valuable advisor. After several plays, I think, strategies emerge as far as splitting your dice up and, especially, blocking and such. Some building rows are much more useful that others, it seems, or, at least, much easier to use effectively. E.g., I can't imagine rolling nine or lower enough to make many points off of the University (as opposed to the Embassy or whatever). Maybe I haven't delved into it quite enough.

The press-your-luck battles can be crucial, as far as devastating your resources or buildings if you undercommit or being worth just paltry points, if you overcommit. It's one quibble I've got.

The expansion, though, is great for expanding the building options, adding some variability to the sessions (and the players' tableaus) etc. etc. I like it much more face-to-face than on the computer. I think there are more enjoyable dice Euros, but this one is quite good. Maybe a touch long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a thematically fun and mechanically solid game, with just the right amount of luck for my tastes, at it's core is a sort of worker-placement-with-dice mechanic.  However, there does seem to be a best strategy, both when placing influence (Can I somehow make my dice add to 14 this turn?) and when constructing buildings, which detracts from the replay value a bit. 

The combat mechanic leaves much to be desired.  In my mind, it either needs to go away completely, or raise the stakes and complexity a bit to make it a big climactic experience.

RATING: 8
Played 4 times. Fun game, but it can be incredibly frustrating too - the dice rolls don't go your way, you get the roll you're looking for but the player before you in turn order takes the spot you needed to be able to build that crucial building, etc. I suppose that's the common tension amongst all worker placement games, but in this game it's especially tough, because you have to also be lucky enough to roll your worker to be able to place them in certain spots. One of the players I played with last night spent the last 6 production phases of the game rolling less than 8 each time (yes he should of built the chapel, but if memory serves, he didn't have the statue, and I'm assuming was thinking his luck would break). He was pretty dejected by the end of it. If I had a true gripe about this game, it's that the game is deceptively long. The first eight phases of the game go by pretty quickly, and you think, "I can do this for four more turns in no time", but each subsequent turn is slower as everyone calculates out what's the most optimum move for them in their production phase, etc. and suddenly you're playing a brain burner and AP starts rearing it's ugly head. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
[ESP] A pesar de no ser una gran amiga de los dados siempre me divierto mucho jugando este juego.

Los jugadores representan a Lords enviados por el rey a administrar sus territorios fronterizos. El juego tiene lugar a los largo de 5 años con un total de 20 turnos. Cada año hay 3 epocas productivas para recolectar recursos, construir y entrenar tropas. La cuarta es el invierno, en la cual todos los jugadores deben pelear para derrotar a las fuerzas invasoras.
Para lograr esto deben usar con astucia sus dados al influenciar a los consejeros del rey y construir los edificios mas convenientes para la estrategia seleccionada.

RATING: 8
Kingsburg is just a solid all-around game.  I don't think I've ever played with anyone who didn't enjoy it.  Drafting your resources based on dice-rolling is a cool mechanic, and Kingsburg does a pretty good job of it.

It seems like generally people can play a variety of strategies, mostly successfully, but it does feel a little silo-ed from time to time.  There's very little direct confrontation, so sometimes it seems like you're playing in a vacuum.

The artwork and the theme are great, but the components leave a little to be desired.  Cubes as resources are kind of lame, and they break the enchantment a little more than I'd like.

One little quibble (and it might be just me) is that the resources feel a little bit too scarce in this game... Or maybe it's just that the early level buildings don't seem to pay off that much.  Most of the time when I'm playing this game, I feel like I'm waiting for a couple of turns down the line when I have a few more resources.  It makes you change your plan quite a bit and it increases the tension, but at the same time it's hard to feel like you're ever doing anything really cool.

The buzz over this one is understandable, but i would probably still go for Burgundy, when having to compare.

RATING: 7
Worker placement game that uses dice rolls to determine where your workers can go. I first saw this implemented in Alien Frontiers, and I very much enjoy how differently it is implemented here. The resource management makes the game very thinky, as does the skill tree of buildings available to each player. The potential to block opponents makes the game tense, and the constant threat of the monsters at the end of each round creates a race for military points. Intermittent rewards for most points/least buildings keep strategy diverse. Impressive game developed around rolling dice.

RATING: 8
You know what? Some days I just really like rolling some bones, and Kingsburg is one of my favourite ways to do that. Kingsburg has you rolling a bunch of dice and having to work with the results to get the resources you need -- basically a "dice placement" game. It looks great, and there are several paths to victory when building your town, which is cool. The end of year attacks add an element of risk-management to your choices, and although the game can occasionally be a bit anticlimactic (in that it can be obvious by the last turn who is going to win) it's still a lot of fun. In fact, Kingsburg has really surprised me with how much I want to keep going back to it. I think it's a really good game.

RATING: 6
This should be a comment of abounding praise and adoration for an awesome strategic game based around dice-rolling.  I mean this game had everything going for it that I look for in a game (theme, fun mechanisms, multiple paths to victory, etc.)  Unfortunately, disaster struck!  I literally rolled 10 or less every single turn of the entire game.  This kind of worse-case scenario didn't really annoy me, but it opened my eyes to a major flaw in this game.  When a player has consistently bad rolls like mine, they can't win.

Now I'm not bashing on the game.  I had a fun time playing, and I would gladly play again.  However the few little mechanisms they threw in to help a player who rolls badly aren't enough to make that player a competitor for the win.  Sure you can try different combos of buildings, but you're going to struggle to get enough resources to build the really great stuff.  It's a fun experience, but when things don't go your way the experience lasts a little too long.

RATING: 6
I've only played this once in the Dice Tower Con, but it left me with the impression that it is a light middle weight worker placement family game with some interesting decisions that doesn't overstay it's welcome. I'm glad that I got to experience this old classic.

There is a lot of dice rolling; although the dice could be manipulated, there is some player conflict in regards to taking actions and there is an engine building aspect that holds your attention. 

I left the Con purchasing the game for family nights; although I could see myself playing this with gamers after a heavy session.

For now the rating is a 7 for fun, but if you roll higher numbers than everyone you'll probably win.

RATING: 7
Dice placement, what an excellent mechanic.  I definitely love this game, and while the temptation in a dice game is to decry your luck,  players end up rolling 15 times over the course of a game, which mitigates the luck greatly.  Plus, one the best spots on the board is also the cheapest (Jester).  Just brilliant game design - adding dice to worker placement is ~exactly~ what that genre needed and the whole package is a ton of fun to play, while still being highly strategic.  Unlike most WP games, you have to "roll" with your dice, which forces you to mix up your strategy from game to game.

The only bad point - the end-of-year monster battles are way too random and really detract from the strategy of the game.  This means one of the game's pivotal moments and decisions comes down completely to the roll of a d6.  Thankfully, the expansion "Kingsburg: To Forge a Realm" fixes this completely.  My rating of "7" is thus for Kingsburg without the expansion.

RATING: 7
The dice placement is interesting to me. The buildings feel kinda "same-y" and this game just feels more abstract to me than other games of its ilk. Not sure why, though. This should be up my alley.

-Edit: looking forward to trying it out with the exp, which might help my issues with the generic feeling buildings and the random battle resolution.

-Edit: The exp fixes my 3 main beefs w/ base game: 
1. Same-y buildings: now more choices, and different choices for each person
2. Battle resolution too lucky: before the luck was coming (strength of enemy) and going (king's roll), now you have control over one of them. Excellent addition.
3. No variable setup: the events, choice of building strips and characters make each player's position, and each game, different. 
I really liked my one play with the exp. May go to 8?

RATING: 5.5
6 plays.  Too long for the amount of fun involved... and too many calculations that could be for 'naught when you get blocked out by opponents.  Details --> Roll dice & decide how to spend them to bribe characters.  Block your opponents from playing their dice to the board.  Poor rolls can really tank your game.  Seems like 3 dice is too few to keep all players competitive... because someone is bound to have really bad luck with the dice.  I hate to think that there might be a single, viable strategy, but perhaps everyone needs to rush to get the "farming" building (which gives you a 4th die for production).  The game seems to have a rich-get-richer problem.  -------------Others say:  middle/light weight game, reminiscent of "To Court the King" but more complicated with more interesting decisions, too long for this genre, no chance for 2 losers to catch up in 5-player game. ---------Update: it's slightly better with 3 players where there aren't as many calculations and permutations to run through.

UPDATE 2014 (at 10 plays): A worker placement puzzle that becomes exceedingly frustrating, painful and tedious... especially if you roll poorly for several consecutive turns or your opponents are playing willy-nilly and not keeping other players in check by blocking.  The dice randomness and limited ability to mitigate it really kills this game for me.  Also, the fixed sequence of buildings is boring.  Variable buildings or building chains would greatly enhance replayability.

RATING: 6
This is a very pleasant dice based Euro occupying the same "head space" as Lords of Waterdeep. I prefer it over Stone Age as one actually develops a little engine over the course of the game. Additionally, play is a matter of managing the dice rolls rather than suffering from the luck of the dice. It doesn't seem to be quite Euro enough to satisfy some or thematic enough for others. Add that to the fact that it has a few years on it and I think it lacks the amount of attention now that it might have had it been released in 2013. I'd prefer a bit more interaction and immersion, but it's a game I can play and enjoy when I'm in the mood for something that doesn't require a full intellectual and emotional investment. 

With the very fine expansion I rate the game a seven. It just opens up the possibilities more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Dice outcome determines worker placement freedom of movement. A bit like an inverted version of Stone Age. Like Stone Age you collect resources but instead of houses you buy upgrades. The upgrade 'engine' not only makes your actions better, but also provides victory points. There's a little added fluff in the form of invasions you have to beat each couple of rounds. These are postive or negative effects that happen when you meet (or fail to meet) a certain value on the battle track.

Kingsburg is pretty light and feels like real fun on the first play. Continuing the Stone Age analogy, the dice outcome here is more prevalent and can seriously hamper progress. Since you're building an engine and must meet certain quota in time throwing low numbers feels frustrating sometimes and the game doesn't have much mechanics for planning for a worst case scenario.

The real weakness of the game probably lies in the replayability. The upgrade paths seem to add variety but there are some staple upgrades everyone goes for. In the end the one that throws the highest dice values has the best chance of winning. That's not a problem per se, but it is for a game that easily plays over 1,5 hour.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
It's light, plays in about an hour but has strategy to some degree.  Since each productive phase is based on dice rolls, you have to be prepared to not be able to execute your plan the way would have wanted to.  It's a very dice-heavy game, and you'll roll plenty of them and curse them pretty often.  The player interaction is non-direct, and really comes down to dice placement.  If you can place dice to prevent opponents from taking full advantage of their roll without screwing yourself over you've discovered all of the player interaction I've found in the game.  It's pretty fun and light, something to play with light or non-gamers.

The more I play this game, the more I like it.  It`s fun, and despite being at the mercy of the dice, it offers a lot to the player and is usually interesting.  Bumping my score up.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The key mechanic in this game is the process of placing influence by rolling 3D6 and dividing the spots among a number of "advisors" who grant favors in terms of VPs or resources. Buildings are constructed at various stages to also yield VPs or other benefits. Turns are phased over a span of four years after which VP totals are determined for victory. At the end of each year a variable number of marauders must be dealt with the planning for which must be included in the player's decisions.

Reminiscent of such games as Settlers of Catan (the dice for resources), and Puerto Rico (construction) with the randomness quotient in between the two. Also similar to Louis XIV. The saving grace in this game that elevates it above Settlers is that the resource generation mechanic is not quite as chaotic and there are devices to give a "tail-end Charlie" a bit of a boost.

Decent light weight game I've managed to play a couple of times and found it held my interest. I'd play it again, but won't likely be buying it...

RATING: 5
Thought I'd like this more than I did :(
Too much downtime, you have to think about where to put your dice, which dice to use and also factor in what all your opponents might do with the dice they have (plus any bonus '2' disks they have) ... meaning each turn takes an age, and you can't plan out of your turn as you have no idea what slots will be free. End of year battle is a massive anticlimax, and seemed too easy to defeat in the game I played. There's no 'push your luck' element here - if you get a bad roll, tough luck, if you roll well then you're half way to winning ... totally tactical, there's no way you can really plan strategically other than the track you build on, but that's totally dependant on the resources you get, which you have little control over. Finally it's way over produced, a $15 game with cards and dice would be fine, but you get a huge board and lots of eye candy none of which adds to the game. Overall better than to Court the King, but I hated that too, so it's not saying much.

RATING: 8.2
The King has challenged us to compete to improve our provinces.  There can be only one!

This is an easily learned on its incredibly lovely and high chrome board.  It seems to interest the younger crowd and in spite of its incredibly flat learning curve offers good replayability.

The point of the game is to roll dice that will give you various levels of "pull" with Nobles working in the kingdom.  You simply place your dice on the matching noble and that claims that Nobel person power for that particular production phase.  

After the claims are made, there are checks made for various levels of balance or accomplishments.  Sometimes those in first place are rewarded, and sometimes mercy is shown to the slackies.

Toward the end of each year an invasion occurs that can be bad oir relatively harmless.  The point is through the game you gain defenses as part of building various compounds in your province.  

The game ends after the fifth year.  Points are acknowledged and the winner is clear on the VP track.

RATING: 7.5
Es un juego de gestión de recursos en el que las acciones se escogen haciendo combinaciones con una tirada de dados y ubicandolos (solos o en grupos) sobre los consejeros del rey. Aunque tiene dados, el azar está muy controlado. El juego se desarrolla durante 5 años y cada año consta de 8 fases. La última siempre es enfrentarse a un ejército enemigo. Las acciones están encaminadas a ir consiguiendo recursos para construir diversos edificios de tu ciudad e ir preparándote para el combate de final de año. Al final gana quien tiene más puntos de victoria.

Es un juego rápido y entrenido. Admite de 2 a 5 jugadores. Recomendable. Puede gustar tanto a jugones como a no-iniciados.

RATING: 6
Kingsburg!  You mothafu*&#$!  This is one game that redefines love/hate relationship.  Ok, so I love worker placement, so I should love Kingsburg.  Ok, the components are beautiful, the play is smooth as can be, so I should love Kingsburg.  And the first week after I got it, I played it every day.  And it was good - kind of.  The reality is, I want to cut Kingsburg's heart out and feed it to the damn parrot fish from Reef Encounter!  Why?  Well, how about - I CANNOT win this thing!  (I cannot in good conscience count that one tie as a win).  I shouldn't care about such a petty thing, should I?  I mean, really, as long as I'm having fun...  But I'm not having fun.  Why?  Because I can't find ways to improve.  I can't get better.  When I look back over the game and try to analyze what I did wrong, I find that - for the most part, I did everything right.  At least as much as my opponent.  My strategy is good, I make no glaring mistakes, my military is never an issue, I am competitive.  The reason I lose continually is - bad dice mojo!  And this is the reason I despise Kingsburg, because at the end of the day, all other things being equal (and they can be, very often), whoever rolls better will win.  I don't think Kingsburg is unbalanced per se - sometimes I think it's too balanced.  If you're having a bad game luck-wise, there is no way to compensate in another area, as it is almost always impossible to pick up more than 3 goods a season (and have them be useful).  So you can't make a big score and build two buildings in a season (except quite early on) without skipping the previous season to save up.  Really, I could go on and on about this.  I can't take the Kingsburg much these days... but it calls to me with that damn siren song.

RATING: 2

---
UPDATE: Kingsburg is essentially just Airships done wrong. Given that Airships now exists in English, there is essentially no reason to play Kingsburg, unless you're a fan of downtime, analysis paralysis, arbitrary luck, and overwrought, underdeveloped games.
---
There is a good game in here somewhere, but the one in this particular box is one developer short of being interesting. There is just way too much thought involved in performing simple tasks. There are so many permutations of options, and so many ways to get hosed, that accomplishing even small things - like collecting a single resource - requires way too much work. Then the proliferation of similar buildings provides many more options, a lot of which aren't viable or interesting. Things just get way too bogged down in analysis paralysis, and the game takes far, far too long for what it is if anyone is trying at all.

Then there are two systemic problems. The first is the battle at the end of the year, which should be exciting, is really boring and hugely random. Second, the game simply rewards getting high rolls too much, even after you've done all that work. Most dice games are about pushing your luck, playing the odds, that sort of thing. This one isn't, it's a purely tactical game where getting good rolls is just a lot better than getting bad ones.

It also doesn't help that this whole worker-placement type game is becoming grossly over-used. It's not a terribly interesting or fun game mechanic to begin with (compared to, say, an auction), and too many games are using it without compensating in any way for its shortcomings (mainly, too much downtime and too little interesting player interaction). I remember Aladdin's Dragons with fondness, and would play that over virtually any of its modern successors any day.

No denying Kingsburg is a reasonably clever game, and the ideas in here could be made into something interesting, possibly. Kingsburg is not that game, though, and I would not play it again. To Crown a King this is not, not by a long shot, despite being twice the price and what, maybe 3 times the length?

RATING: 6
I love dicey games and this one takes dice and uses them in a pretty neat way.  While your rolls largely determine what actions are available to you, there are other elements in the game that allow you to bend the rules enough to where there's usually something useful you can do with your rolls.  There's also a sense of progression as you move through the game, building better buildings and fighting nastier bad guys.  However, it appears after many plays that there's pretty much one clear cut strategy that you can use to win almost every time.  It would be great if there were different building combos that were competitive, but there just aren't.  This takes Kingsburg from a great game to a very mediocre one after you play it a few times.

RATING: 3
Played it twice, but I really don't see the appeal.  I'm not a huge fan of dice, generally, but I thought they might work on this occasion.  They did not.  I didn't feel that the decisions were particularly interesting.  

Rarely were the spaces I wanted/needed blocked.  The number of advisors is overwhelming relative to the number of resources, so there was typically a workaround to get what I needed even if the first choice was occupied.  The proliferation of +2 tokens, a building that allows +1/-1, and the King's white die all provided excessive flexibility, which only served to reduce tension. 

The building powers seemed unbalanced (although I can't say so definitively without many more plays that I'm unlikely to pursue), the building costs seemed close to arbitrary (perhaps because the resources themselves are all so easy to acquire that a 3 gold, 1 wood building feels roughly the same as a 1 gold, 2 stone, 1 wood building, or 1 gold, 3 wood building, and so on).  The fact that the same building sheet gets reused every game would appear to detract from replayability and result in set strategies (I would have preferred a Puerto Rico style building market, so that players could at least compete for buildings, producing variation due to scarcity).  

Perhaps the most disappointing aspect of the game was combat.  The attacking monsters are severely underpowered, and the knights sent by the king (determined by a die roll) were usually enough to dispatch them for the first few rounds without any player investment at all.  This really reduces the players' desire to use actions relating to combat, making several spaces on the board little more than overpriced defaults.  Moreover, the cost for losing is typically taken out in resources, but in our games, players simply spent all of their resources on the turn before the Winter, meaning that the penalty for losing (in the case of a bad roll of the King's die) was simply not getting the one resource cube prize and/or the one victory point for the largest army.

The art was pretty, if cartoonish, but the whole thing felt like a protoype of a game that would eventually be awesome with many more refinements/additions and much more playtesting.  It's short enough and innocuous enough that I'd play it to appease someone, but otherwise I don't see much there to excite me.

RATING: 7
Oh, Kingsburg. What a pleasant game you are. There's something about placing your die rolls to influence one of the king's advisers that is just so satisfying. The entire game is like this. You get resources to build your town at a drip feed though, so choosing what to do next isn't always straightforward. There's a gentle, euro-like level of aggression with other players where you can block and snatch up advisers before they can, leading to the need to be comfortable with modifying your tactics on the fly.

One negative I do have with it though is the relative lack of decision-making in preparing for the incoming monster hordes at the end of every year. It's very easy to see their strength before it arrives and dedicate a turn to bolster your forces to fend them off (or not and just take the hit for losing) I've heard that this issue is fixed in the expansion, along with adding a lot of other interesting choices, so I'll definintely be picking that up.

RATING: 7
VG to LN
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Kingsburg is the latest in what seems to have turned into a trend of dice-centred games. In this particular case, players are, in essence, given a mat depicting a bunch of different buildings, and 3 standard dice. Each round you roll the dice, and then use them singly or in combinations on the board to gain resources from the 18 characters present. Each character is numbered 1-18 (1 for each number, inclusive) and can have only one player present. So essentially this is the worker placement thing from Agri-Cay-Pill-da Vinci-III using dice. The resources are used in a similar way to the numerous other games as well, you collect them, and then use them to erect buildings which break the rules in various ways. At the end of each series of 3 rounds, a random bad guy from a deck shows up, and you need to have invested enough in military strength to defeat them, or face a minor penalty.

The bottom line on this is that I'm already way over my limit on this type of game, and adding dice to the mix did little to increase the appeal. It's not horribly random, though luck certainly comes into it, but it is just too derivative for its own good. On an aside, the board is garish.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Kingsburg is my second-favorite game next to Through the Ages. It's surprisingly playable with kids and casual gamer, but especially with the expansion, it's plenty strategic. 

It'sa mix of a resource-gathering game and craps. You roll three die and then literally place one, two or three die on spaces marked 1-18. Those spaces have resources you collect and with which you build buildings, plus you must get military to fight the monster at the end of each of the five rounds.

Another reason I rate this game so highly is that it offers buildings and a board that somewhat alleviate the damage caused by bad dice rolls. Also, I enjoy permutations and combinations, and the game never plays twice because you can go so many different directions with which you build buildings on a 5 X 5 chart.

RATING: 8.75
Each round you roll your dice and assign them to various advisors, but if a space is taken, you have to arrange your values differently or you're out of luck.  As you progress in the game you'll gain resources to buy buildings which improve your abilities considerably.  But once a year there's an invading army to deal with - have you mustered enough defenses?

Outrageously easy to teach considering how complex a game it is.  I do understand the cries of balance issues, and we felt when playing it that a consistently poor roller can have a tough time catching up to the other players.  But even with those problems, this was a clear winner in our house and will get lots of play.

RATING: 8
This game is based on worker-placement, but the workers are dices, that you roll every turn and then select the person(s) that will help you this year. The trick is that all the others players can't take the person that you took, so you can sometimes block them.
The art is nice, there are a lot of components in the game (and the bags were included!). 
The thing is that my gaming group likes it more than I do, can't say why.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Components: Beautiful board. The various advisors are all very cool looking, and the other pieces of information (season track, score track, player order, number of soldiers, year marker) have all been well integrated around the edges. The invader cards are cool, and the player boards have clearly defined powers on them.

Experience: Great fun. Resources are very scarce, and it's easy to block my opponent from selecting a particular advisor, which forces us down creative paths.

Keeping up with my military is a must, as the penalties for losing a battle become crippling as the game goes on. This is perhaps a problem with the game, that military is so important, and yet there aren't too many ways to achieve it.

There are several buildings that are more valuable the earlier a player can build them, so the game sees an interesting and frantic race to get these built as quickly as possible.

I'm a bit concerned that there are some obvious building choices (e.g. Market) that tempt opponents into very similar builds. Not sure how much differentiation will surface after repeated play. I look forward to using the expansion to mix it up a bit.

Update: using the expansion's larger player boards doesn't seem to help much, as the newly added rows seem less valuable than the pre-existing rows. Market is still a no-brainer, and I'm not seeing much in the game that forces me to do things differently.

First Played: 2010-06-07
Last Played: 2015-05-24
Plays (11):
2 players: 6
3 players: 3
4 players: 2

RATING: 7
Played this several times with a variety of players and find it easy to teach and fun to play. It's a pseudo worker placement mechanic, but is also about managing your resources based on others' plays. The Geek has an excellent strategy guide that should be read. You have to plan ahead and take some risks, especially in the early rounds. Drive to the end of one of the tracks and reap the benefits, then use those benefits to garner even more points. Points are at a premium in this game and it is usually a tight score. The game can run a bit long, 2 hours is usual and if you have some high AP-prone people, it can bog down. But I like the mechanics, the art and the interactions among the players. The exapnsion only serves to improve the gameplay.

RATING: 6
The game is a fast and fun worker (dice) placement/resource collection game.  You can start out with a general strategy on what building paths you want to follow, but sooner or later the dice are going to dictate what strategy is actually available to you.  That makes it more of a tactical game, especially when considering how to place the dice to help yourself while hindering your opponents.

It is best played as a light game; over-analyzation can really cause it to drag.

I've played this a LOT on the computer, but I only log my face-to-face plays.  I was worried that I wouldn't enjoy the game as much due to the time commitment as compared to the digital version, but after several plays, I've come to enjoy the hour long gaming sessions.  It's a nice, relaxing and fun game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
Worker placement with dice as workers. Players throw 3 dice and place them on 18 action fields (separately or combined). If one player has chosen an action field, another cannot use it, except if he has gained the king's envoy (given to the player with the lowest number of buildings in his town). Actions consists of gathering resources (stone, wood and gold - not lemons!!), trading resources, recruiting soldiers, taking a peak at the enemy which will have to be confronted at the end of the round, gaining +2 dice modifiers, and gaining points. After the actions have been done, one can build 1 building. There are 5 types of buildings, which can be expanded three times (20 different buildings). Each building offers specific advantages.
The game is played over 5 rounds, each consisting of 4 seasons. During spring, summer and autumn, workers are placed and buildings are built. During winter the enemy has to be fought. The enemies become stronger each round. A player with more battle points than the enemy gets an prize, a player with less battle points looses resources and/or buildings (and in the latter case probably points). The objective is to gain most victory points by means of worker actions, acquiring buildings and winning battles.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Kingsburg is a simple-to-learn, resource management, town-building, character role selection game. Players build up their tiered town board by getting goods from a wide variety of characters, each with their own role. This is not the thematically rich role selection like in Citadels; each character simply amounts to a different mixture of goods and services, but the interesting part is the method of selection. Each player rolls a number of dice and then combines the die rolls however they like, and places some portion of them on one of the characters, numbered from 1-18. This provides the corresponding resources and also blocks that square from all other opponents. Players take turns doing this until all of the dice have been placed and then the building phase begins. Each player uses the resources they obtained to purchase one of a number of buildings, each of which  provides victory points and a special ability. The goal of the game is to obtain the most victory points by the optimum usage and blend of the mechanisms described.

I like the following elements:
*Its simplicity and approachability
*The dice placement mechanic
*The character role selection
*The building upgrade paths

I do not like that the following elements:
*Character roles are not very thematic (I'm a thief so I trade a victory point for some bags of goods?)
*Some roles seem imbalanced (I know a 17 is statistically harder to roll, but three victory points and a battle card peek and two goods of your choice?)
*Some buildings seem imbalanced (the Farm gives you an extra die to roll for the remainder of the game).
*It is difficult to build an economic engine when relying almost entirely on random dice rolls.
*The game takes longer than its simplicity warrants, sometimes due to analysis paralysis.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Pretty good but flawed entry-level game. Nice for family play. Designers take note: If you design a game in which higher dice rolls are preferable to lower ones, do not count on the luck to even out, especially over only 15 rolls. The luckiest dice roller should win this game. A few mechanisms attempt to mitigate the effects of luck, but later games do the job better.

RATING: 6
Had a lot of fun playing this game.  Our 5er game moved along at a pretty good clip.  A little AP, but not much.  It took us 2 hours to finish which felt about right given the nifty tech tree.  This is one of the games I'm not sure how well it would work with fewer players.  The best/funniest part of our game was when the selection you wanted to make was taken by another player...or when we tried to psyche another player into taking a certain spot.  "I'll take the 12, wait...it's not my turn...ok you go...but not the 12 please".  The main weakness I saw is the battles.  You roll a die and add that to your combat strength.  Greater than the monster is a win, less is a loss, even is a tie.  While winning or tieing is not a big deal...losing can be catastrophic.  So the difference between a win or a loss can come down to a critical die roll.  We rolled a "1" in a key battle, and it cost one player a level 4 building he had just built (a VP producer).  A "1" was probably the only bad roll for him and sent him from the penthouse to the outhouse just like that.  Very tough to recover.  Last, I'm not sure how many different strategies (on the tech tree) there are.  This game may become stale w/repeat plays.  If so, I'd probably drop it down to a 6.

Update: I have played this a *lot* online.  Finally got a 2nd play in face-to-face.  This one has lost its shine.  Lack of different strategies is the main reason I no longer want to play it.  Maybe I overdid it with the online plays.  Dropping it from 7 to 6..probably should make it a 5.

RATING: 6
Players roll 3 or more dice each, and take turns assigning them to roles on the player board.  Roles assign out goods, military aid and extra abilities.  Goods build buildings, granting special powers; aid fights off foes at the end of each year.  It's all about gaining victory points for buildings, abilities and defeating foes.  Nice growth game that plays intuitively and very well.  May be a touch too long for the gaming payback, but it's not a big deal.  I enjoyed this game but have a couple of concerns - you do have to roll well to have a chance of winning, some of the buildings seem very powerful (the Town Hall/Embassy in particular), and the end of turn enemy didn't really seem that terrible.  I like the game, but hopefully the expansion will tighten things up a bit.

RATING: 10
Number 1 in my heart :D

I love love love this game.  The game board is absolutely gorgeous and the components are top notch.  I've yet to play this with someone for the first time and not had them stare interestedly at each piece as we pull it out for setup.  

It's incredibly easy to teach and pick up so I don't find you have to 'go easy' on new players the first game.  Every person I've introduced it to with my husband has found the game fun and competitive.  At first glance, the game seems simple.  However, it's incredibly strategic.  Knowing when to go for the statue, or locking down position 1 is crucial to being successful at the game.

This next statement is probably what makes me love this game the most: for a dice rolling game, you are not relying heavily on luck.  An opponent can roll 17 three or four times during a game, yet if you understand what buildings to go after, they don't necessarily win.

I could say more, but I think everyone who would read this has played Kingsburg with me and is well aware of my love for this game

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Weight of 2.40, so it isn't too hard to learn, another worker placement game.  Check the video review for more.

Impressions after one play:
1. The dice mechanism complicates things and creates analysis paralysis.  Rather than just focusing on making a decision from the choices available to you (as in other worker placement games, like Agricola, Stone Age, Pillars of the Earth), you have to do some brain burning figuring out which options are even available to you in the first place.  This creates downtime and I'd rather be using thinking time to be deciding on which option is best, rather than figuring out which options are available
2. The fighting at the end of each round adds a risk element to the game: do you play conservatively and perhaps "waste" precious resources to build up a solid defensive base of soldiers, or do you focus on expanding your kingdom and getting victory points, at the risk of losing big?  Is this element of risk management good?  I didn't care for it.  I don't mind games with luck (example: Thebes), but here the luck can trump good strategy.  It can have too big an impact on the game, and takes away from the strategic decision making in the rest of the game.
Overall: there are better worker placement games to choose from.  But I'd like to play this again with more players as part of 100 in 25 Challenge.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Kingsburg was a lot of fun for a while. I got over two dozen plays out of it, a lot of them on BSW while traveling for work, but I couldn’t be persuaded to play the game now what with Troyes on my shelf. I think it was a nice design for the period it came out, but it's been superseded. I played most of my plays with the military rule from the expansion, in fact, we played with that having learned of it on BSW before the expansion was actually released, and I think it's the only way to play or else a player who ignores military can get help too easily from the king. 

The problem with KB is that there isn't enough dynamism in the game in a couple ways. First, there's no way to mitigate high vs low rolls (as opposed to Troyes where you can buy other players' dice or Castles of Burgundy where 6 is not higher than 1). Second, we always went the same way, up the military track first and hit the top track for VPs last. Our games came down to us all pretty much doing the same thing and just seeing who rolled better at doing it.

The expansion tries to address these problems with event cards and different building tracks that you can lay over the original building tracks, but the events just added more unpredictability. I can't say I played with the new tracks enough to comment on them, but the fundamental problem of high vs low dice rolling is still here. I appreciate KB for the fun it gave me, but I'd much rather play one of the new dice strategy designs such as Troyes or Castles of Burgundy.

RATING: 5
Based on 4 plays with 2 players, and 1 play with 4 players. Setting the issue of dice aside for a moment, the game gets into a groove fairly quickly when playing with two. It's considerably better with 3 or more. I wouldn't recommend Kingsburg for less than 3 players, as otherwise it overstays its welcome. You can learn the strategy quickly, so you need those extra players to liven things up. 

01/19/2010 Update:
Funny how playing a new game, can prompt you to drop the rating for another. After playing Airships, Kingsburg has dropped in my estimation. (Two points) The problem with Kingsburg, is that the dice dictate what you can do. There's little tension, and no real "pushing your luck" element. In Airships, the tension is introduced when you choose what to go for BEFORE you roll. This simple change makes a world of difference.

RATING: 7
Allocate dice to draw resources, in order to build buildings and resist the monsters coming in Winter. Clean, elegant game.

Duration: Fast, little downtime.
Design: Played with dice, the board, a couple of counters, and the monster deck. You react to luck and try to block your opponents; interaction is limited to this denial mechanic.
Strategy: The major choices are how to allocate your dice, and which buildings to build.
Theme: Fun fantasy theme that fits nicely.

RATING: 5
Kingsburg is a dice roller's version of a resource conversion game. Ideally, in such a game it wouldn't matter much whether you roll high or low. Kingsburg does have a lot of options, and many ways to mitigate bad rolls, but high rolls are still far superior. This is because of the much reduced competition for the high spaces and because, when you do choose to hedge your bets, you'll still lose against those who don't and simply roll well when defending the realm at the end of the year.

Supposedly, the expansion fixes at least some of those issues.

RATING: 7.5
It's a simple game that plays quick, is tense and has some simple blocking interactions. Since the only thing that changes between games are the invaders, I imagine that it will get old rather quick. The strategy is rather obvious and rolling high is almost always better, so it's quite luck-dependent. It's a bit fiddly, there's a lot of powers that need to be triggered and remembered, which really hurt its status as a family-friendly gateway game. I enjoyed the game, it moves along nicely and it's always tense to roll the combat die at the end of each year, but the game feels underdeveloped. Hopefully the second edition will alleviate this.

RATING: 7
Played twice, four players was better than three players.  There's not enough interesting variation in what the different people (numbers) offer you.  It seems like the lower numbers need to be stronger than they are so that splitting your dice up is more profitable than lumping them together.  Also, there's probably too much information to analyze for such a light game - if I go here, she'll have to go here and then he'll be stuck with this unless she goes there instead in which case . . . .  Finally, the attack at the end of each year didn't seem to be threatening enough to inspire fear.  The game was decent fun, though.  I would play again if invited, but wouldn't ever suggest it.

[UPDATE] Played a couple more games that included the expansion. Wow, the expansion addresses all of the game's weak points and really kicks Kingsburg up several notches. I'm raising my rating a point as a compromise between the so-so base game (rating=6) and the excellent expansion (rating=8).

RATING: 7
I have had some concerns that there are only a few different viable strategies to pursue and with repeated plays this view has been solidified. That does not matter that much because there are many small tactical decisions in the game which does make up for it. The game is quite long for its strategic hollowness, but that honestly don’t detract from the fun of the game. The turns go relatively quickly except when there are some tough decisions.

So what’s the main attraction of the game for me?  At least when this came out, in most games with dice, the decisions were trivial. Not in this game as there usually seems to be more than one way to place them, especially considering the psychological aspect of what other players plan are. You can play this game as a competitive game, but it also suit well for casual plays.

I think this game works best for three or four players. With just two players it seems to lose tension and with five players there can be problem with over analysis as there are many combinations of the dices. With more players this game is more cut throat as more dices are placed on the board. The second row is more important in those games because it gives more flexibility.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Seems like an interesting game in theory, but in practice, several fatal flaws show themselves:

1) The supposedly balanced die rolls aren't.  Low die rolls force you to spend several dice for a single resource, especially when traffic clogs up the lower numbered advisers.  High ones get you almost a guaranteed 1 die per resource or better.  Roll a few fives or sevens back to back, and you might as well forget playing any further.
2) The rich get richer as their buildings protect their other buildings from the monsters.  Meanwhile the poor get poorer as they randomly lose more and more buildings and find it harder to protect the ones they have.  The production-helping buildings are interesting, but no one can focus on them, since the battle-helping buildings are so vital.
3) The "catch-up" mechanic doesn't nearly compensate, since getting the resources to build two buildings at once is next to impossible after the first column is done, and the extra die can fail you as easily as the first 3 dice do.  The doubling up on one adviser is nice, but an extra resource or two each year won't get you back in the game.
4) Being forced into losing your "best" building when you lose a building is crippling.  Late in the game, you've just blown several turns of resource collecting and a turn of building with nothing to show for it.  Letting you keep the victory points (or some fraction thereof) from a destroyed building would at least soften the blow, as would letting you choose the building that's destroyed, within some parameters.
5) Too many combinations of dice and action choices cause extreme Analysis Paralysis.  (Do I add my  2 here, and use my inn there? If I choose wood&gold here, I have to swap gold on 6, but if I choose stone&wood, I have to swap wood on 6, etc.)

Overall, I'd need some heavy convincing to make me try this one again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.7

RATING: 7
One of a new wave of Euro games that feature an interesting die-rolling, worker-placement mechanism.. this time you are building a town over 5 years and trying to protect it from marauding monsters at the end of every year. The artwork and components are really nice, and the gameplay is fun, with plenty of tough decisions and screwage.
Points are scored for various buildings, and for fending off the monsters, as well as from the various advisers used to obtain resources throughout the game. 


Although a decent game in it's own right, it is let down a bit by the mechanism of the King's reinforcements, which has been completely changed in the expansion by including soldier tokens (the the rest of the expansion is pretty good too, but this part is essential)

RATING: 3
IMHO, the player rolling the highest will win the game.  What gets built is very similar between players, so the strategy can not be dramatically different, thus the higher die rolls get you more stuff sooner.  The invader mechanism tends to be either a non-issue due to the king's roll, or just a roughly equal effect on everyone.  Since the player with the most buildings gets a VP, and the player with the most buildings somewhat gets the most armies, the leader can really pull ahead, while the catch up mechanisms only help the last-place player.  Thus, the only real tension can be avoiding last place.I understand this is $60 in the US - way too much for what it is.

RATING: 7
Easy to learn, and diverse appeal. RATING: 6
Low interaction game with a lot of luck and a serious tendency towards analysis paralysis as people feel the need to work out what every player will do before performing their turn.  Its also about twice as long as it should be.  

Our game was surprisingly close for a game where dice rolling and the chaos of the other player's decisions can screw you over easily.

Despite all my problems with the game, its very easy and can be pretty fun.  The different buildings give each player different strategies to try out and the stuff going on is well balanced.  The components are pretty nice but the theme is tacked on.  The dice rolling mechanic is very cool and 3d6 allows for a nice bell curve.  If you build the re-roll building early the luck can't hose you too bad.

OK game for people who want something that isn't too complex, but not enough replayability to satisfy most.

RATING: 3
After one play, I'm not likely to play this again.  There is way too much dependence on dice rolls -- if someone consistently rolls high and gets to pick the Queen (17) or King (18) many times, they will win.  If you're stuck rolling 7s or 8s, it takes 2-3 turns to get the resources that they get in one turn, and you will lose.  Furthermore, the game can slow down a lot as each person tries to figure out the possible partitions of *all* the players' dice rolls, the motivation of the other players (that is, what numbers are they likely to try to claim), and how to block them while maximizing your own use of your dice.  This is not an interesting problem; it's tedious and rather random.

Someone said it was like Caylus combined with Yspahan.  I agree -- it has the weaknesses of both games.

RATING: 8
There is more game here than meets the eye! Gameplay is balanced, regardless of the fact that one rolls 3d6 at each production phase.  The die rolls act as a limit to your actions, as you can get from 1 to 3 "rewards" each turn.  The bonus " 2" markers and the Farm's extra white die work very well in this system.

During our first 4-player game, two players had not focused on scoring victory points, thus the spread was large.  The second game saw a result of 36-35-35-32, with the player in last having dropped from 37 due to failing a combat against the dreaded Dragon.

Feels like a mix of ROMA, adding multiplayer capabilities, and CAYLUS, where resource management is key.  Player interaction (i.e. blocking) is limited to the die placement on the Influence board.  Players can build what they want on their "land" (board), assuming they have the required resources, and follow the building "tree" (which is very straight forward).  The rules are easy to learn, and the game can be thought quickly.

This may just be the best light Euro in my collection!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Kingsburg is a simple, but fun, dice game.  I am not certain if it is considered a "gateway game", but it certainly could be used as one.  It is simple to teach and play.

Each turn, you will roll between 3 and 5 dice (usually 3 or 4).  Then, based on the numbers rolled by your dice, you can pick an adviser or advisers to gain resources, victory points, etc.  You can also block your playing partners from obtaining these spots for that season.

The game is fun to try to balance resource and/or victory point gathering with building up defenses.  There is risk here, so that adds a nice element, too.

The only downside is that I always feel like rolling 3 dice (or 4...and rarely, 5) is just not enough.  This is not really a problem with the design of the game, per se, but rather just a general feeling that my family and I get throughout the game.  In other words, we want to do more with our turns.  Also, while the defense mechanism adds value, it seems like something is lacking just a touch in this department.

Overall, an enjoyable and simple game that does not take a lot of time to play.  Also, while players are somewhat at the mercy of what you roll, there is definitely strategy involved in dice placement and what you choose to build.

RATING: 7
This dice-placement game is a clear forerunner to Alien Frontiers.  Unlike AF it offers a far widwe spectrum of choices for your dice; you are unlikely to not have anywhere to apply a rolled die.

It's a smooth-running Euro- accumulate resources, gather troops or build buildings, collect your VP.

It does strike as a little themeless (despite the attractive fantasy setting, the dice don't represent ships or anything recpgnizable as they do in AF), but it is a well-thought out game.

It seems many gamers feel that Kingsburg tends to play similarly after a while; I will want to add in the expansion set shortly and compare game variability.

Starting this at a 7 after (1) solo play.

RATING: 2
Dice-rolling yahtzee-fest. Poor rolling = poor game. That's how my first and last game of this luck-driven nonsense of a game went. It's a '2'.

Update (06/05/12): I was wondering why I enjoyed Castles of Burgundy so much, although it is another dice allocation game. The difference is that in CoB, the die rolls represent relatively [i]equal[/i] opportunities. Grabbing a one hex building may be more helpful, than selling goods, or placing another building on the tableau. In Kingsburg, the higher total rolls represent progressively better and [i]superior[/i] opportunities. Rolling 3x 6s is a rare event, and is arguably the best opportunity. Rolling 3x 1s is also rare, but is the worst situation. On the other hand, rolling 2x 1s in CoB does not carry the same meaning. It may represent an opportunity to pick up something in the '1' area, or put a building in a '1' spot, or sell a '1' good. One is not necessarily better than the other. How you discern the relative importance of each choice, and how you use those choices to leverage into more opportunities is the strength of CoB. Kingsburg is thus, arguably an inferior design that pastes a theme on luck-based results; you may as well play Yahtzee.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Another game, that didn't make the cut into making a (semi)permanent home in my collection due to never hitting the table. From the "solo" instances I played(4 ghost players essentially) the game has really good mechanics and doesn't seem like it would be hard to teach new people. 

Dice allocation is a nice mechanic that simulates worker placement to an extent, one of my favorite game mechanics. The game is made even better with it's expansion, "To Forge A Realm," which should be purchased without hesitation if you own the base game. 

Happy to hear that a reprint is on the way(2017) which incorporates "To Forge A Realm" expansion, and a bit more to boot. May pick it up again, because with the expansion, Kingsburg is amazing

RATING: 5
OK, firstly this is what the designers intended it to be: light fun. Unfortunately it's still one pip short of a good roll. The problem is that rolling high is much better rewarded than rolling low (which is why an extra die is used as a catch up mechanism). Some of the prizes in the 18 squares are decidedly odd, with gold easier to get in quantity than wood or stone, which means getting the right combinations for buildings is harder than it should be. Bizarrely, the queen space gives a better reward than the king space. Finally, the various catch up mechanisms mean that scoring is tight, so winning comes down to luck on the final turns.

RATING: 5
A lot of people like this game, and it has some mechanics I really enjoy.  Everyone likes building buildings that build on each other to give you nice abilities, and the resource management is accessible as well.

The biggest flaw in this game is the degree of randomness.  The dice placement is creative, however unlike many such games it is almost completely unmitigated: I don't care if I go first if I can only take one crappy action I wasn't going to lose out on anyways.  The foes that attack can be very harsh, which is fine, but all it takes is a series of bad dice rolls to be left totally aware of your fate and completely unable to change it.

These things would not be flaws in a somewhat more casual game, however it plays up to 5, and with 5 frequently takes two hours, which is quite a commitment for a game in which defeat can be so easily snatched from the jaws of victory.

RATING: 8
This game is FUN! It's POSSIBLE to get screwed by the dice, but you'd have to roll really, really badly. Otherwise, the built in balancing features more than make up for it.

You get the most resources by splitting your dice up, but people who don't split their dice up will obviously suffer the most from low rolls.

Mostly a fun worker placement game, but finally a game that makes the actual placement of workers an interesting game in and of itself.

RATING: 7
Based on one play: feels similar to Stone Age (the components are gorgeous, the basic gameplay is very simple, dice are combined with a worker placement mechanism). On first sight it lacks Stone Age's tension (or elegance) but has more different paths to victory (meaning it is more complex). The first play kind of dragged, because we were just trying to understand the usefullness of the advisors and building tracks. I think with repeated plays the game will be played faster and the subtelties of influencing the advisors by placing the dice will shine through. In that regard I feel it might be more strategic and interactive than Stone Age and might give more bang for the buck for the same game length. Also it plays with 5 and has an expansion for more variety.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First played December 17th, 2008. Taught in 20min, played in 90min. This game has great luck/stragety ratio. The game is played over 5 years(round) of 4 season. Each player is given a player board to keep track of territory upgrades. The goal of the game is to have the most VPs at the end of the game. During the first three seasons in a round, each player rolls their dice to influence members of the council to gain resources. The lowest total is the first to place their dice on the game board and places die(ce) on one concil member and then turns are taken clockwise until all have placed all of their dice they wish. Then resources are awarded and you may build one upgrade per season. At the end of each yeas, you must fight invaders. Depending on if you are sucessfull or not, you can stand to gain or lose quite a bit. I'd strongly recommend giving this a go and consideration for purchase.

RATING: 6.5
Traded Dungeon Petz for this plus expansion... mistake!
Kingsburg is similar to Alien Frontiers, rolling dice and assigning them to gather three types of resources.  When the dice rolls go against you, this game becomes hell.  Slow and extremely repetitive with little strategy, but holds a high degree of screw you (particularly against those who have rolled low die results).

Expansion helps with a bit of variety but can't overcome the dice rolling faults of this game.  Whereas Alien Frontiers is an excellent gateway game, Kingsburg is not.

RATING: 6.5
Ideal Players: 2-5

Description: Roll your dice, and create sets to influence advisers. Collect resources to build buildings and defend against attacks.

Pro: Indirect conflict. Every round is an interesting puzzle to work out. Different building tracts have different advantages. Very little downtime. Beautiful art.

Con: Luck has a huge influence on the game. How you roll plays a large part in whether you win or lose. Theme pasted on the mechanics.

RATING: 7
Simple mechanics; moderate strategy; easy rules.  Very approachable for gamers/non-gamers alike both adult and teens.

While my rating for this game started with a 9 I have adjusted in after playing numerous games of Troyes, a dice rolling/worker placement game I have found to be far more strategic, replayable and rewarding.  Still love Kingsburg (and think the expansion is one of the best ever), but my tastes have changed (evolved?).

3/22/14 update:  just played with my 10 year old for the 1st time, and found two things to be true - very easy for this age group to pick up and that the game is not anywhere near as fun with only two.  The two of us played so I could teach her the game, but from now on I will never play with less than four.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I liked this game but I played with the expansion which I heard fixes an issue or two (roll for king is one) and gives some more good choices so I'm unsure what I think of just the base game alone, I believe my rating should be close and with expansion about a 7.  

I really liked the different options on the main board and on the player board, some paths did seem significantly better than others but more than one way to skin the cat for sure and your decisions are meaningful for sure.  I Like the tech tree aspect and how there are a few paths to choose though I think the expansion helps here also. It seems even when rolling bad you still have some choice though rolling bad often will mean your chances to win will be much less than your opponents, over the course of a game it should mostly even out but not always. There are also ways to mitigate bad rolls which I liked, including some cards from expansion. I was surprised there was no end game scoring variant, even in the expansion. Expansion almost a must have for way more options and some fixes, the base game without would stale fast I think like 3 - 5 plays, with it not sure but many more for sure.  

The biggest plus might be it can work as a gateway and still keep a more avid gamer interested, very straightforward rules and play, turns move fast and the game is fun. Biggest knock is probably the length, 90 minutes would have been a great length, 2 hours is a little long for what it is. I don't remember off hand now how tweakable this is.

RATING: 6.5
Reasonably enjoyable game but it seems like there's really one way to play it. A full military strategy doesn't seem viable, it seems like your best bet is rushing a farm while making sure you can just barely beat the monster, and then work on getting as much of the top row as possible. I guess that's fine but for me for a game to be great I have to be able to do different things from time to time. Also the king's reinforcement die is absolutely terrible, the expansion is probably worthwhile just for the soldier tokens (though most of the other modules are also pretty cool). But yeah, probably a bit too much luck, though that's not such a problem with a lighter game like this.

RATING: 4
Light-weight die-rolling game where players roll dice that allow them to influence various councillors. These in turn get the players resources. Resources are then spent on purchasing buildings, and these yield VPs and special abilities. Once each turn a common foe is revealed, and each player individually has to match the strength of the foe or suffer dire consequences.

My one experience with the game wasn't too good. While there are a lot of likeable and innovative mechanisms in the game, the combination of prominent die-rolling (resulting in quite a bit of luck) and rather slow and plodding gameplay doesn't work well, and I would really have liked the game to have been a turn shorter. I don't really have any desire to play it again, hence my low rating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
Rating after two plays, which I won.  I'm still trying to figure out why I like it better than Stone Age so much, or if it's just that warm fuzzy feeling of 'getting' a game.

Here are my possible explanations:

1. You roll BEFORE you make your choices.  This makes it a more interesting optimization problem for me, where you deal with what you are dealt, rather than making some choices and then hoping for good luck as in Stone Age.

2. The choices and placements of you and your opponents actually affect each other.

3. The buildings are more interesting.  Huts are all identical.  Resources in SA are all identical.  But here, the resources you get makes a more noticeable difference on how you'll play.

4.  Is it just the art?  Am I more affected by random fantasy art than a bunch of brown pictures of neanderthals?

Edit: I think this is going on my "no real desire to own but willing to play" list.  It's fine, not exceptional, and perhaps a bit overdone for a month there, without getting more interesting with repeat plays.

RATING: 4
2012-01-30

Initial Rating: 5.5 (January 2012)

At first I liked the game, but after one play I prefer to play Alien Frontiers, both games have a healthy dose of luck, but if you roll low numbers in Kingsburg you are toasted while in Alien Frontiers you can still do a lot. This is one of the few games I really wanted to finish soon because I was bored with it. The game can be long and the theme is almost nonexistent, a weird thing for a Fantasy Flight Game. I liked the end of turn attack, but the enemies are easily defeated and there are some buildings that are obviously stronger so all the players go for them. All in all not much strategic depth on this one but can be good to introduce to your family. I think I will play it again but this is a game I won’t actively pursue.

Current Rating: 4.0

RATING: 7
I don't know what to think... my first impression is that it was just a more complicated version of 'To Court the King' (which I like).

Initially, I didn't like the game much, but I'm coming around.  It's a little fiddly and the end-game is very anticlimactic.  Last round... the winner is determined!  There's no end of game scoring whatsoever to offset any mistakes made during the game.  There appears to be multiple ways to victory, but a string of bad dice (i.e.-low rolls) and you can get shut out early on, with no possible way to catch up.

I originally gave this game a '5' but, after playing the Java version a few times, have updated the rating to a '7.'  The game also plays well on BSW.

2017-11-06 - This is still a really good game, but it's fighting for table time with other games that I just prefer. This is still the best entry to dice placement, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The dice mechanics remind me a little of [gameid=16496]. Okay sort of game. Easy to teach.

The dice can totally mess with your strategy and I'm not sure taking the cautious road is a viable alternative, if there is one.

To mitigate luck, Kingsburg uses catch up mechanics, dice rerolls and one time use +2 dice modifiers. I don't feel this gives enough control back to the player. For example, in a 4 player game, opponents can unintentionally block other players just because of the dice combos. Certain spots can go beyond a player's grasp at vital times with no ability to setup a good secondary strategy, just in case. The +2 dice modifiers mean if you roll only odd or even numbers, there is limited ways to get to odd or even spots (only thru buying a specific building). Rolling high gives a serious advantage to the player simply because it is unlikely they will be blocked and it guarantees using all 3 dice. 

The end game can be anti-climatic since their is no hidden scoring component. Everyone knows where they stand simply by looking at the track at any point during the game.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It might be worth learning Kingsburg against the AI, but the experience is nothing like a real game of it. 

This is much, much better than Stone Age or Pillars of the Earth. And just because you're rolling dice doesn't mean it's not a straight up minion allocation game. The dice are supposed to make it a lightweight in the genre, but in fact they make it more interesting than SA or Pillars because there are constraints on everyone's placements. The building tracks aren't bad either - certainly better than Endeavor. 

Seven rating is only if the expansion is used. Game has too many drawbacks without it.

RATING: 7
Roll your dice, usually three, and place them on the numbered spaces on the board based on adding the value of the dice together. Higher numbered spaces give more resources so this introduces some luck into the game. Resources allow you to build different types of buildings. After a few rounds of this, there is a monster to fight. The hard choice is whether to focus on building your military to clearly beat the monster, or build up resources, but live closer to the edge for the fights.
