RATING: 5
A solid effort that seems to be lacking a certain spark to propel it to greatness.  The dice mechanism is nice, as long as it doesn't bog down (how long does it take to evaluate your options?).  I like the unexpected nature of the end-of-year invasion, which must not be underestimated.  I fear that there may be an optimal path to victory, so hopefully further games will show that there are different viable strategies.  Right now it seems like you first get your military up, and then worry about building prestige buildings.

Update: Like Stone Age, I never had the desire to play this game.  There's nothing that I really dislike about the game, but it just seems boring and I can't imagine myself wanting to play this game instead of the others I already have.  Traded away.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played twice at BGG.CON.  I think the placing dice mechanic to be rather clever, and amazingly it seems balanced.  However, there is one dice roll that I feel will be a problem--the King dice roll.  Somewhere you'll need to gamble on the outcome of the King's dice roll.  If you spend your resources to build up your army, and the King throws low, then you'll probably win.  If you spend your resources building buildings and the King throws high, then you'll win.  With such nice mechanics for the dice elsewhere in the game, I was disappointed in the die roll here to determine such a big part of the game. 

I think the game works better with 3 players than with 5 players.  If you throw low in a 3 player game, you still can get a couple of resources.  If you throw low in a 5 player game, you might be stuck with only one resource.

RATING: 8
I consider this an improvement over the Settlers of Catan hedging your bets mechanic. Whereas in Catan one can easily be outplayed within the first 5 minutes of the game by suboptimal beginning placements (especially for people that haven't played before or for people who don't understand probability well this can be a real issue) this game essentially gives players the ability to revive from the ashes repeatedly, as well as gives losing players lots of chances to get back in the game. The game I played in was very close - I have a feeling most games will be very close. The rules are learned pretty quickly - there's a lot of small steps to the game but it's pretty easy and intuitive to figure out.

I've heard the expansion fits well with this game and would like to try it out sometime soon.

RATING: 6.5
Played once, and I think there are some big flaws. I immediately spotted what seemed to be the only strategy that truly makes sense. Get the Inn as early as you can. That extra die every season is huge, and far outweighs the -1 in battle it costs you. With this one thing, you can get more/do more each turn, and I got ahead early and never looked back.  But then, no one else went this route; might be different if other players leveled the field. In that case, you probably should just roll dice for a while and declare the high roller the winner.

And yet, I found it quite enjoyable. But I suspect that's just because I was winning throughout.

A second and third play, and I was convinced that grabbing the Inn early has an opportunity cost, as going that route really hampers you in the battles. Luck of the dice is still too high, but the game is a bit deeper than I first thought.

RATING: 7.5
First thing- get the expansion- it's half of the game Kingsburg should have been from the start. 
This is a nice, simple euro game focused on managing your dice. Every turn you roll three of them and place either as a group adding the numbers or seperately on poles with coresponding numbers. When you place dice on a pole nobody else can use it- this is where most of the interaction comes from. These poles provide you with suplies that you need to build your town. I think the game should be played with timer because most of the final score depends on your calculation skill and with right amount of time you are able to make almost no mistake in placing dice in which case the interaction becomes only theoretical. Also for what it is I think the game should take no more than two hours and it often does. It should play smoothly as it really is a medium-light game it just doesn't feel that way.

Kingsburg has light medieval theme and fairy-tale artwork. It looks much better than most of such games although the game board looks campy. Everyone has his own city sheet on which he can build buildings that he wants, and these sheets though simple look really nice.

RATING: N/A
The game takes place over five years, a total of 20 turns. In every year, there are 3 production seasons for collecting resources, building structures, and training troops. Every fourth turn is the winter, in which all the players must fight an invading army. Each player must face the invaders, so this is not a cooperative game.

The resources to build structures and train troops are collected by influencing the advisers in the King's Council. Players place their influence dice on members of the Council. The player with the lowest influence dice sum will be the first one to choose where to spend his/her influence; this acts as a way of balancing poor dice rolling. Even with a very unlucky roll, a clever player can still come out from the Council with a good number of resources and/or soldiers.

RATING: 6
[2012.Feb.14] A solid game that uses dice in an interesting manner to generate resources. That said, my biggest gripe--which left me feeling 'meh' about the game in the end--was that if any one player consistently rolled well (i.e., 4s, 5s, & 6s) over the course of the game, then he/she would have significant advantage over the other players. In most games, the well-known bell curve helps smooth out these types of die rolling anomalies. However, once in a while one player will have Lady Luck on his/her side. When this happens, the game becomes rather mechanical and obvious (and I've been on both the winning- and losing-side of this effect). I don't hate the game, but I certainly don't love it either.

RATING: 5
The use of the dice in this game is very clever. But I am not sure if there is really en interest in splitting the dice on various space. Most of the time, people ended up using all their dice on 1 space. I think more space would have been used if players could get more than 3-4 dice. The 17 and 18 space are also rarely used.

The game is ok, it's not particularly annoying to play, but it could be enjoyable. There is one thing I could not stand and it's the building.

In most games I played, making a building seems like rewarding. For example, in Puerto Rico, you are always happy to gain an new special power. It's interesting to see what special powers you could get and you could try to focus on gaining a particular building.

But in Kingsburg, they are all boring and this is what kills the game, because most of the things you do in this game is to acquire resources to make buildings. And because the buildings are so boring and useless to make, you simply don't care about building them. So in the end, yo don't care about the game because that is the main objective.

Many abilities of the buildings are either repetitive, useless or unappealing. I think they should do more their math because some of the buildings have odds as low as 1/18th chance of being used.

I think they should place more time redesigning their buildings, it might improve the game. I saw that there was an expansion, maybe they did it.

RATING: 7
Alle Spieler haben 3 Würfel und würfeln diese simultan. In aufsteigender Reihenfolge der Summe der Würfelwerte setzten wir die Würfel ein, dabei dürfen wir unsere Würfel beliebig kombinieren um höhere Berater zu beeinflussen. Je nach Berater erhält man verschiedene Bonies (Ressourcen, Waffenstärke, Sondereigenschaften, etc.).
Mit diesen Dingen kauft man sich Gebäude auf dem eigenen Tabloe, die wiederrum Eigenschaften mitbringen.
In einer Runde wird 3 mal beeinflusst und Häuser gebaut und danach müssen alle Spieler gegen einen Gegner antreten, der die Verlierer bestraft und die Gewinner belohnt.
Mir gefällt das Spiel sehr gut, das riesengroße Manko ist der Zufallsfaktor im Grundspiel, dass bei einem Angriff am Rundenende jeder Spieler einen W6 als Kampfstärke dazu erhält. Dieses Manko wird in der Erweiterung behoben.
Die Erweiterung lohnt sich aber auch sonst, denn es gibt individuelle Hofpläne, Gehilfen in Form von Karten und und und. Das Spiel wird durch die Erweiterung besser.
Das Grundspiel ist, abgesehen vom Kampf, aber sehr gut wie ich finde.

RATING: 6
Strangely addictive fast-moving [b]dicing[/b] game. Since I want to play 'just one more turn' or 'just one more productive season' I have to add a point to this rating. What a truly awful name! evocative of absolutely nothing (no on ever remembers which game this is by the name of it).

I wish the spread of buildings was larger, with more variety. The lineup of buildings is occasionally odd: military buildings lead to the school of Wizardry?? ...for example. The lack of text on the main board makes the game longer as people try to figure out what is going on in there. And I hate when my military units go away each turn and I have to start fresh!

By examining this game piecemeal I could chalk up a number of shortcomings with the game (including especially the awful name), but that would be unfair because as a whole the thing works nicely for me. It's a 'sum is greater than its parts' game.

I have to reduce my rating by a point due to the non-English text which removes a lot of the [i][b]feeling[/b][/i] and thus theme which could have been part of the game. I marked up my copy, but shouldn't have had to. The game is too long, also.

RATING: 7
Only played it a few times 2 players.  We I like the game.  We do a variant to the two player rules.  We add a third single die so 3 spaces on the board are taken by the ghost player.  If two roles are tied, we put them both on the same space.  This leads to more scarcity which is good.  There are a lot of dice and some luck, but not as much as you would think because there are a lot of different directions you can go.  The key is being adaptable.  I think the luck may come more in the military roles if a player is taking a more risky strategy and another isn't.  

Note: This game improves a lot with the To Forge a Realm Expansion.

RATING: 8
This game falls pretty firmly within the "plays itself" category of Euros, and thus won't please purist fans of the genre who disdain die-rolling and want to be able to indulge in long-term strategizing. For the rest of us, the game is hard not to love. There's lots of drama, quick reversals, and cursing the gods, and the way that each of the games five "years" builds up suspensefully to a battle with NPCs is clever and totally original. It's also gorgeously and lovingly illustrated, with a playfulness that stops just short of self-parody.

RATING: N/A
This review is worth reading so as to make play easier: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/328797/kingsburg-a-detailed-review

Some consider it a gateway game, others do not.
90 min playtime and can play with 2

Family friendly acc'g to some: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/619581/kingsburg-and-bgg-not-a-family-game-and-a-lower-ra
(BUT at potentially 2 hours gameplay, it could end up no being too much fun).


See one person's comments: "While Kingsburg is a great game, and I’m glad I own it, I do not consider it to be a “Gateway” game exactly. Namely because of rule fiddly-ness, numerical analysis and playing length.

1. Rules – when you hand the non-hobby gamer a veritable “spreadsheet” of buildings, each with it’s own rule, you might just blow their mind a little.
2. Numerical analysis – When you roll your dice you have about 7 potential placements (14 if you get an extra white dice with your roll). But then add in the modifiers (+2 tokens and Farms) and you just might have a bunch of AP zombies to deal with (instead of the ones on the cards).
3. Duration - Newbies are going to take 90 minutes to 2 hours or more (depending on number of players, mainly because of #2 above). Gateway games usually clock in at half that time.

The average weight given to Kingsburg on this site – 2.43 – is well ahead of other lighter games generally regarded as gateways. I’d consider Kingsburg more of a “next step” game."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Received today (2/20/08) but on the shelf until Pandemic gets a play.

Went through the rules, and decided that this definitely needs a players aid for new players learning the game.  5 years, 8 phases in 4 seasons?  It is a bit much to hear.  I almost think that the "seasons" lingo could just be left out.  EvanMinn's Player Aid served well.

The game has attractive art, and moves smoothly.  I like it more than "To Court The King" because the player moves are quite transparent.  In other words, everyone takes their turn to perform their actions, and there aren't so many manipulations of the dice that need to be carefully audited (as in TCTK).  In my games of TCTK, there have been people who weren't cheating, but just misunderstood or misremembered how to use the powers.  And yes, they usually won!  Kingsburg does not suffer from that same problem... I like its design much more.  Although my initial rating will be a 9, this has the potential to move either way after a few plays.  It also depends on how much fun is had by the people I play with!

UPDATE:  After another play, it gets even better.  The game moved quicker.  Three players:  initally led by red, but they didn't protect for a winter phase and lost the Church... never recovered.  Yellow looked solid, but started losing the King's Agent (?) to red and the most buildings and most military bonuses to blue.  Blue got the 9 VP building to catch up, then a building that allowed disposal of a good or "+2" for a VP each production round.  Blue graped the lead, but to do so had to take a bit of a risk, and had only 5 total military going into the final winter.  It was the 7 pt Barbarians, and the King sent 4 soldiers.  Blue won by 2 VP's.

RATING: N/A
In Kingsburg, players are Lords sent from the King to administer frontier territories.
The game takes place over five years, a total of 20 turns. In every year, there are 3 production seasons for collecting resources, building structures, and training troops. Every fourth turn is the winter, in which all the players must fight an invading army. Each player must face the invaders, so this is not a cooperative game.
The resources to build structures and train troops are collected by influencing the advisers in the King's Council. Players place their influence dice on members of the Council. The player with the lowest influence dice sum will be the first one to choose where to spend his/her influence; this acts as a way of balancing poor dice rolling. Even with a very unlucky roll, a clever player can still come out from the Council with a good number of resources and/or soldiers.
Each adviser on the King's Council will award different resources or allocate soldiers, victory points, and other advantages to the player who was able to influence him/her for the current turn.
At the end of five years, the player who best developed his assigned territory and most pleased the King through the Council is the winner.

RATING: 4
I've heard the designers wanted to make a game that made every dieroll worthwhile, something they didn't find in Settlers. In the first place, they missed (there were often rolls in our 5-player game that weren't very productive). In the second, it's not a problem in Settlers because there are many dierolls over the course of the game, and the turns are quick. Not so here, where there are only five full turns in the game, with three "seasons" with a dieroll each per year. Too often you have to wait a bit to decide what another player will choose before you can determine your own play. Might be better with fewer player, because less interaction might actually help the game.

RATING: 7
The first time I played Kingsburg, I kept expecting (despite having heard the rules) that there would be a traditional Euro-style reckoning at the end, so I wound up dead last. After several plays, I still wish there were some hidden points -- it's just disheartening to know you're behind and have few ways to catch up.

It's not the game it sounds like: From the name, I thought maybe it'd be about 14th Century accountants working in cobblers' shops, or something, but it's really kind of like Warcraft: The Boardgame. Very RTS-like, and the combat is a nice twist.

Solid across the board (hah!), with a lot going on and plenty of tough decisions -- especially tough decisions that have to be gambled on for several turns. The way it uses dice is brilliant: random in a fun way, but nicely mitigated by giving you good options no matter what you roll, and by providing ways to up your odds.

---

After some time without any strong desire to play Kingsburg, I consider it a good game but its rating has gone from 9 to 7. When I think about suggesting it, I remember that it has no hidden scoring and I suck at it, so I spend most of the game knowing I'm going to lose. I wish it offered a way to keep everyone in the hunt.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Do not waste money on the french version, as I grabbed the english version of base+exp for half the price.

RATING: 4
2 / 5 - Fair.

I think this is nicely themed and there's some genuine tension in picking allocating resources to the military or to building. However, if anything I've played is truly deserving of the title "multiplayer solitaire" this is it - the only way to influence your opponents is to  get to adviser spaces before they do and whether or not you can or would want to do that is entirely in the hands of the dice. There is also a cringingly obvious path to victory which effectively leaves everyone using the dice to race up it. Were that not enough the game is full of catch-up mechanics that don't entirely mitigate a runaway leader problem and your economic analysis can be, and often it, sabotaged by the dice. See my review for details: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/315003
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.57
Two biggest cons is that having 18 places to place dice feeds into the random factor determining who wins. If you keep rolling 7-10 between three dice, there's not a whole lot you can do. We had a run away winner and he said the strategy is simply roll high.

Second con looks like very low replay ability. When all said and done it is still a battle of VP and the highest pathway is with the top row on each player board. Second highest is the second row and similarly down the line. This means the path to victory is rather well laid out with no real further investigation. The monsters attacking balances the VP march to a point, but not enough by itself. I would like to play it with all of the players being experienced though. I think part of the issues was having two new players playing it.

I liked this quite a bit despite the lower scoring. It just needs tighter choices with the dice placement and more diverse player boards.

Rating Potential: Likely stuck at 6.5. I don't see it being a 7 and it's enjoyable enough for 2-3 plays to keep it from being a 6.

RATING: 3.5
OK, so this game is beautifully produced and I like the theme. Allocating your dice rolls and blocking/being blocked by other players was great fun and "seems" to give the illusion of strategy within the luck of the dice rolls. However, I'm just not buying it! Your village contains a number of buildings that must be built in order (like a skill tree) to gain benefits. But, there are only so many resources and so much time to build. Defense and the ability to change your rolls a little bit are really important. Which means that everyone builds pretty much the same buildings, with the leader maybe having an extra building. It likes a slow plod to rolling one great round and being able to build that one extra building. It reduces this game down to dice rolls on a pretty board. Plus the AP of everyone counting each others dice is killer (cause really, what's the fun if you can't screw with your opponents)... I really wanted to love this game but it is pretty terrible, especially with 5 players.

RATING: 7
This game works beautifully as a second-stage gateway game and yet has enough strategy and multiple paths to victory that keep more-experienced players entertained.  I love that the dice are used as a means to get resources, but that it's your strategic placement of the dice that determines victory.  Definitely a "make the best of the luck that's given to you" type of game, rather than "you roll poorly and you get screwed".  Sadly, after many plays it felt somewhat stale as we'd all figured out our favorite strategies and only slightly varied them from game to game.  The "To Forge a Realm" expansion has been a HUGE boost to this game and has given it a second life within our gaming group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A good dice placement game taking place over five years. Each year consists of three seasons where players roll and place dice to gain favours from the various people on the board (each spot can only be claimed by one player), and once all dice are placed and favours are claimed players may in order choose whether or not they want to build a building this round. At the beginning of each season a bonus given either to the player ahead or the player behind in buildings depending on the season. Each year culminates in fighting the looming threat, and rewards/penalty is given to players depending on whether they ward off the threat or not. Whoever has the most points at the end of the game wins.

It's an enjoyable game, with plenty of options in terms of building up the points you need. Though the queen's favour is a significantly powerful spot to claim in my experience. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I thought this was a fairly interesting game with a nice balance of luck and strategy, with multiple paths to victory.

I enjoyed the dice rolling and the screwage factor. Both made the game more tense and enjoyable. I played a five-player game with only one experienced player, and three of the four newbs (including me) were very competitive.

I like the luck factor, because it forces you to think on your feet and means you can't just go for the perfect strategy. You have a lot of options for placing your dice and can opt for blocking other people's moves, if you want.

Of all the Euros I've tried, I think Kingsburg, Small World, Ra and King of Siam are my favorites. Dominion, Age of Empires III, Taluva and In the Year of the Dragon, on the other hand, seem less interesting in retrospect. Mind you, Kingsburg is not a great game, and I don't expect to ask to play, but having to play it again wouldn't make me ill.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2008-05 A mostly new twist (well, similar in idea to Ysaphan) on role selection/action drafting where players roll dice and then split them or add them to select various roles.  The roles generally provide resources which can be used to build... um, buildings.  Although the game does have two catch-up mechanisms and they aren't extremely powerful they are definitely needed because the leaders have an advantage in having more buildings (which give various powers) and being able to earn bonus VPs (for having the most buildings every round).  Get too far behind early and it's a real struggle to catch up.  There's also a build up your armies to fight the incoming hordes at the end of each round mechanic which has been used in a few other games.  It works and although I would be happy to play it again I feel a shorter version would improve the game.  Could get old quickly -- perhaps a expansion with a new twist could help the sameyness.
UPDATE: 2012-07 played again in 2011 with some enthusiastic players and enjoyed it.  Raising rating a half point.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Played once.  This game involves using the numbers on rolled dice to choose similarly-numbered spaces on a board, which gets players one or more of three types of goods that let players build things to get victory points and defend themselves from regularly occurring attacks.  

While the game is a fine medium-weight game, the major problem I ran into was that it was insanely long for what it is.  On each turn players roll 3d6 and then can use any combination of those (and possibly +/- 1 or +2 of any combination of those) to choose from one of 18 spaces on the board.  Each space can be entered only once, so planning your moves is key and takes a Very. Long. Time. (as players must both look at all permutations of what they can get with their dice as well as all permutations of what their opponents can get with their dice, and factor into that what's already been taken).  And, since all the choices are based on dice rolls, and your opponents' moves can drastically change what you wanted to do, it's difficult for players to plan ahead.

Added to this tedious placement mechanic is that players can choose from 20 different buildings to build with the goods they get; each building (of course) gives different powers.  So, not only must players ponder all the permutations of their rolls, they must also consider which of the many buildings they want to build (all of which require different resources).  

While this would have been a fine ~1 hour game, our game took well longer than 3 hours.  It's just not worth that much time.

RATING: 4
Kingsburg is yet another worker placement and resource management game, this time featuring some trendy dice action. This game was worth playing a few times via the excellent Java implementation available here on BGG. Variant rules and differing player counts force you to consider slightly different strategies to start the game, and then you puzzle through fifteen snappy rounds (AI = zero downtime) to see if your strategy works. In this format, it takes as long as a game of Dominion or 7 Wonders and offers up about the same level of challenge and types of decisions. Not bad.

Playing live against real human opponents unfortunately offers little to recommend itself over the plethora of other similar games. Lords of Waterdeep, Homesteaders, Macao, etc. ad infinitum. Place workers. Get resources. Build buildings. Rinse and repeat for 90 minutes or, in this case, two hours. Kingsburg is just more of the same.

RATING: 2
It all comes down to the monster card.  If all players are roughly equally skilled then the game is almost completely random.  You either pick the risky strategy, the medium strategy, or the conservative strategy.  You flip the monster card over and see who is going to win the game.  You're either ruined by the monster, or you've wasted resources being conservative.

The expansion replacing the white defense against the monsters dice doesn't help.  You still gamble on beating the monsters and the riskiest without getting killed will win the game.

RATING: 8.5
In Kingsburg verkörpern die Spieler Lords und versuchen am Hofe des KönigsEinfluss zu sammeln. Dies geschiet durch Würfel, die am Anfang jeder Runde gewürfelt werden. Mit dem Ergebnis der Augenzahlen, lassen sich nun die 12 unterschiedlichen Personen am Hof des Königs beeinflussen. Da die Spieler reihum entscheiden und sich gegenseitig die Personen streitig machen können, ist die richtige Strategie wichtig. Anschließend erhält man Rohstoffe oder Soldaten und kann Gebäude bauen. Am Ende eines (Spiel-)Jahres müssen sich dieSpieler auch noch gegen eine Monsterarmee zur Wehr setzen. Ein bisschen Glück, Taktik & Strategie und Worker-Placement steckt in diesen tollen Spiel!

RATING: 6.5
Fun game that is easy to explain and moves along farily quickly (assuming no analysis paralysis). It seems like a blend of Caylus (worker placement and building), Yspahan (dice roll allocation) and Louis IX (influencing courtiers). Deciding how to place your dice is the fun part of the game.

The only downsides I can see is that it invites analysis paralysis (of which I'm often guilty), and a few bad rolls at the start can make it difficult to catch up. A lot of the game decisions about which "tech tree" seem pretty mechanical/procedural and it's mostly a game of working out how to place your dice efficiently.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Each season, everyone rolls three dice. In player order, the players put the value of the dice onto advisors to get their benefit (earn goods, VPs, soldiers, or look at monster cards, etc). After each season, everyone can construct buildings, which give further benefits and special abilities, but advanced buildings can only be constructed if the lower level buildings below them have been built. (There are 5 rows of buildings, some give benefits with dice, some give VP, some give soldier benefits, etc). Finally, in winter, a monster attacks. A dice is rolled to determine the player's strength, and each player adds their soldier level to see if they defeat or lose to the monster. Victory usually means 1 or 2 VPs, defeat could see you lose a building. Play over 5 years. 

It's an engaging game, with it's fare share of luck. The mechanics aren't new, but it is fun, and keeps you busy over the approximate 90 minute time period. There are various paths to victory to try as well. The luck part is where one can consistently get high rolls, as opposed to one who gets below average rolls, which can hurt you. Your bad luck could take you out of a game early, and that can really detract from the game. 

I do like the various strategies that one can try, and each game can be fresh and different.

RATING: 6
Kingsburg is better than its contemporary, Alien Frontiers, for a variety of reasons, most importantly in how each player's dice placement is not necessarily done all at once. This provides players further down the turn order track the opportunity to place a least one of their dice before all the good locations are blocked up. This forces players to pay attention to what is happening on other players' turns and creates a level of anxiety with regards to ones options disappearing.

The end-of-each-year enemy attack is a frustrating feature, but one that forces players to not just focus solely on engine-building.

I'm still not sure whether or not I actually like Kingsburg, but I'm positive that it is better than Alien Frontiers.

RATING: 8.5
An interesting, creative dice game. The dice serve as place-holders and blockers, gaining resources and preventing others from accessing specific advisors. This, to me, is a very innovative use of dice. This game is certainly luck-based, but a player who rolls poorly can still succeed, if he applies his rolls properly. There are also ways to minimize the luck factor (+2 Markers, the Inn, the Chapel, the Market, etc). A lot of the time the best player wins, but bad luck can disrupt even the best laid plans.

Addendum: luck is a limiiting factor, and a good player can win with bad die rolls. This game gets better with each play. It is also one of the most fun to play games out there.

RATING: 6.1
KINGSBURG - Un gioco italiano, e solo per questo merita attenzione, anche perché effettivamente è un bel gioco. Molto carina l'ambientazione e i disegni che l'accompagnano, parlo del tabellone e delle carte. Complessivamente risulta essere un gioco semplice e tutto sommato veloce, anche perché le partite volano senza troppi tempi morti.

Dal mio gruppo di gioco è stato definito un Monopolone... forse eccessivo, ma c'è del vero.
A me però nel complesso è piaciuto.

RATING: 5
Let's start with the good thing: a new mechanics (dice rolling, splitting the dice and assign). Stop.

Bad things: 
- the mechanics seems smart, but it is basically open only to the "grab now what you can" strategy. Apart from the first year where you can make some speculations on what the other players will choose based on their dice, in the other 12 seasons you have no idea of what others will take (thanks to dice splitting, market and +2 tokens). Strategies are so open that you can be lucky and being left with the things you want with your second die or unlucky and throw your dice away. This no-strategy really annoys me and this is the reason I do not like the game.
- the buildings rows remind me of many other games, but they are not so well diversified (as, for example, Caylus' favors) nor their ending building are so much powerful to invite players to go deep into the row (look for example at the Cathedral with its weak power)
- the theme is non existant. It is not enough to draw a good board with nice pictures to attach a setting to he mechanics. Everybody in this games "takes the 5" instead of "ask for the sergent's advice".
- finally, the winter battles are ridicolous. They become really problematic in year 5, for the rest its just enough to hire somebody in year 4. If they become problematic earlier it's because of very poor die roll... but can a game being influenced in a so crucial part by a SINGLE die roll? The variant http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/366312/kingsburg-winter-reinforcements-expansion makes this part of the game much better (raising my score to +1).

RATING: 7
An enjoyable growth game with a dice controlled placement mechanism. Allocate your dice roll scores to various advisors to obtain building materials or militia. Use your materials to build buildings, which help you build more buildings or improve fighting capabilities. During the winter season of  each of 5 years, you have to fight a monster and if you lose you take victory point or building penalties, so you have to balance between the two areas of growth. In the game I played I lost against the final monster by one point, whereas others just won by one point and that caused me to slip from 1st to last place, which seemed a bit extreme, but still quite an enjoyable game.

RATING: 6
A good game marred by two key problems:

- Unlike games like Settlers of Catan, Agricola & Power Grid the victory points aren't strongly linked to anything; they almost seem tacked on.

- The end-of-year battles are too random. There are two linear random variables at work (the king die and the strength of the foe) that are stacked against each other, amplifying the random effect. The penalties for losing some of the battles are huge, but putting all your resources into soldiers (for little effect, when a 6 is rolled on the kings die) can hamper your building efforts significantly compared to players who didn't bother and relied on the kings die to save them. Too much of it is down to luck.

RATING: 5
Dice games need to be fast. This one ain't and it's poor as a result. Everyone rolls their dice, and you're trying to split 'em up and allocate 'em to as many of the spots numbered 2 thru 18 as you can (taking turns, first on a spot gets it). These'll give you resources used to complete buildings on a tech tree, which give you more dice and abilities to manipulate them amongst other things. You want to maximise the resources you get each turn, which means checking out what other people have rolled, working out if they can take a spot you want or not, can you get multiple spots or should you just combine everything and settle for as big a spot as you can get. It's just not that interesting turn after turn. You'll expect to build over half the tech tree in a game, and their effects are usually minor, so that part doesn't provide much interest either. It might be better when played with more than the 3 we played it with. It's allegedly much better with the expansion which provides more variety in the tech tree. But as one of my game brothers said, why pay double price for an average game when you can buy two good games instead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Unrealized potential. 

The art is attractive, the components solid, and the  gameplay is streamlined. What's not to like? Unfortunately, lots of things. 

Simply put, the game lacks any real tension. Each time we played, I felt a vague sense of dissatisfaction by game end. It was like hanging out with an attractive guy, only to discover he's really shallow and boring. 

My primary problem lies with the dice. I love the little critters, don't get me wrong, but in Kingsburg they're used in a rather tame and uninteresting manner. If you intend on including dice in a game design, they should sparkle. When they're rolled, they should invoke excitement, anticipation, and maybe even dread. The player should feel something; anything. I'm not expecting a profound, life-changing experience or anything, I just want to care about the outcome. 

Maybe the word I'm looking for is "tension." If I was required to choose what I wanted to roll for ahead of time, rather than allocating the dice afterward, the tension would have ratcheted up several notches. 

"But wait," you may exclaim, "then it would be an entirely different game! It wouldn't be the Kingsburg we've come to know and love!"

That's exactly my point. Kingsburg is tame. That's one of the most damning things I can say about its design. I didn't feel any tension about the upcoming attack, and the competition for favor among the members of the court felt rather low-key. 

Parting shot: I don't do tame. If you're including dice in a game, or using any kind of randomizer, then make sure that it adds tension. The line between tension and chaos is rather fine, like the edge of a knife, but it's well worth walking.

RATING: 7
A very unique worker placement game using dice that includes a nice city building aspect.

Pros:
* Beautiful game board.
* Dice placement mechanic is unique and works well.

Cons:
* The designer(s) is clearly a social justice warrior unrealistically making the "master hunter" a female - stop putting this nonsense in games please.
* The King's reinforcements are far too random. (Fixed in the expansion)
* The player boards do not have recessed places for your building tokens, allowing for them to get messed up too easily by simply bumping them.
* Without the expansion the game gets to repetitive.

RATING: 8
Build your city to be great and able to withstand the attacks each winter.

The core mechanism of the game has every player rolling three dice simultaneously, lowest total starting out.
Each player in turn places one or more dice on one of the advisors (numbered 1 to 18) at the King's court.
When all dice are placed (or no more dice can be placed), the players gain the rewards of the advisors from the Jester (1) to the King (18). These mainly consist of wood, stone and gold to build your city, hired soldiers for the defense and straight up victory points.

Then the players have the opputunity to build in their city in order to gain VPs, add defense and other effects.

This is done for three turns called productive seasons (spring, summer and fall).

When winter comes, some kind of attack takes place. The players can see the approximate strength of the attack  beforehand and some advisors also let you take a sneak peek at the attack, which means you know exactly what's coming.
A single die is rolled and added to each player's result. All players that have a total strength higher than the strength of the attack gains a reward, lower is penalized and with a draw nothing happens.

At the end of the fifth year, the player with the most points win.

The dice placement mechanism has the players figuring out what spots are possible to reach given the dice they have. The lower numbered spots are more efficient but easy to reach, which means that more players probably can reach them with a single die. This adds the need to look at the other players dice in order to figure out if there are useful spots on the board that noone can reach but you. If that is the case, that placement can be deferred in order to take a more contested space.

Since the board is static at 18 spaces, gameplay gets a lot tighter at higher player counts. Still, the shifting player order makes it fair, since the last player to go has the highest total and probably has at least one spot noone else can reach.

It looks cool, the gameplay is fun, but there's too much work and not enough reward.

RATING: 6
In this game, your dice are your workers. On each turn you roll your dice and then you can assign the results to numbered action spaces that match the numbers you rolled, or you can combine multiple dice to add their sum to take a higher action on the board. It creates a nice tension of trying to block players from taking their actions by getting there first and making you think about the benefits of one big action or several small actions.

The game also gives you a development board in front of you, which improves various things you do in the game through unlocking slots in ordered rows. Unfortunately some of these rows are better than others, which hurts replayability a little.

:star: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 5
First 4-player games revealed both the good and the bad. It is easy to play, initial buildings are easy to build, and intentional screwage is minimal. Poor die rolls, AP, and (perhaps) a few optimal-strategy builds can result in: a hopeless position, excessive delays, and limited build strategies (respectively). These latter factors may push the rating down to a 6 (yep, the rich-get-richer did it). Overall it plays too slowly and it even 'feels' long... not good.

With the +2 chit Inn, Market, & Farm gives an option for the Queen 50% of the time (a natural 17 on 3 dice is only 1.4%) and, with a little Soldier management, risk of loss is minimized.

The 'Monster' problem can take up to 1/3 of the game and make the outcome dependent on 1 or 2 dr (rating drops to a 5). And, on a pro-rated value of the buildings, the loss penalties are 4.3x worse than any reward; this is ridiculous. A solution would be to make 'Armies' permanent except for changes due to (now infrequent) loss of buildings while deleting the' King's Aid' and any losses, in VP,  would be the difference between the 'monster' and the number of armies.

Next time I will ratio the total of all die rolls to the VP.... see how close it comes to being a constant.

There are possible balancing ideas but why bother with so many other games to play.

RATING: 2
A worker placement game with extremely limited choices.  The better you roll, the more resources you get.  Those who say rolls don't matter are fooling themselves.  There is also little decision making involved in placing the dice, because you are always going to place where you can get the most resources.  The only thing to think about is that you may want to go one spot before another, so another player doesn't block you from that spot.  But the fact is, higher rolls get you more stuff, period.

There are choices to be made on the building track, but most of the tracks aren't worth going for.  This part of the game is simply engine building, and if you rolled better, and got more resources, you will build faster and have more advantages than your opponents.

I don't like random die rolls and I don't like engine-building games.  Put the two together and you have a horrible, horrible game!  How is this fun?  I don't get it.

RATING: 7.75
The simple mechanic of rolling the dice and "deploying" any subset of your dice to the board is really what this game is made of. The rest of the game is slightly below average and your run of the mill resource gathering, building and so forth. The artwork and theme are good but this game is a heavy Euro disguised as light Ameritrash. Player Interaction is limited, which would be my biggest complaint. Maybe allow players to trade, steal, switch resources / dice or else. Apart from the dice roll, no luck is involved which works fine in the game but I do prefer some luck in my games. At least, the success is not accumulative and the tide can change rather quickly, unlike in so many similar games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.1
FINALLY picked up 7/12/16, and in a local trade no less!

Just fun with a capital F. I had read about tons of games on BGG for 7 years, and Kingsburg was the first game I read about that I immediately put to the top of my "Want to Play" list.
[b]And it didn't disappoint.[/b] THAT's saying something, for me. So many top-rated games have such obvious unbalanced aspects (PR's Small Market, early Quarrys and Warehouse/Factory, San Juan's Guild House, Notre Dame's Park, PoF's Professions/Recruiters/Jesters, T&E's Red tiles, etc etc etc) that it was refreshing seeing so many viable buildings and character choices in Kingsburg. 
The only no-brainer seems to be the Queen, but she is hard to get, so that's a big factor buffering her unbalancedness )also, the house rule to fix her is to make her only worth 2 VP's).

[u]On the luck factor, and comparisons to Stone Age:[/u] 
There are multiple buildings (Market, Farms, Chapel) that mitigate the luck factor in KB somewhat.
Further, the 3d6 probabilistic curve is there to likely result in middling numbers that - *gasp* - you then need to CHOOSE where to put them, amongst steep competition for spaces from the other players. And +2 chits modify that further. Heck, I can look at the monster in Kingsburg, and use that information to help me *gasp* plan. Heck, we can all even use the alternative rule and play with the tokens which eliminates completely the King's Aid random roll.

But now we come to the biggest reason why Kingsburg arguably has LESS luck than Stone Age:

Because in Kingsburg, you roll your dice and THEN make your choices.
But in SA, you make your choices, and THEN are screwed over (or rewarded) by the dice.

You can't make any decisions once you've rolled the dice in SA - you are at their probabilistic mercy.

RATING: 7
Added 16mm plastic dice with digits instead of pips to my FF 1st edition copy. The dice that come with this edition are wooden with very rounded corners--easy to knock off the number that was rolled when moving them around the board. Also, the yellow dice have white dots that are very hard to read. The new dice have square edges, so they're easier to keep in the number rolled; and the digits are easier to recognize than the pips.

I used to rate this game 8, and was very enthusiastic when I first got it. For over a year, though, it's been beat out by other games that I've acquired. Finally played it again with 5 players, and I've decided to lower it to 7. What puts me off about this game is that it's difficult to keep track of the timing of the effects of the abilities of all of one's buildings. There is no help on the board, filled with 20 buildings, for when the effects apply. I'm thinking of getting some small stickers for the seven different times when the effects of buildings can take effect.

For me, then, it's design flaws that I think hamper this game. I understand they've addressed the dice problem, which is good, but I think a second edition redesign of the player boards in which the buildings each have an icon or color distinguishing which season they take effect in would help.

RATING: 9
After 2 plays I am rating this a 9.5, and it just may well hit 10 - only time will tell.

Kingsburg is a fantastic game that has both euro and ameritrash characteristics.  I personally enjoy the gradual construction of the province's buildings as the game progresses.  As season's pass, you collect resources through die rolling and placement.  Resources are used to build buildings which aid you in some way.  You must also be mindful of the invasion which occurs each winter - if you have not properly recruited soldiers or completed defenses you will suffer the consequences.

Update 12/31/09 : After 8 plays I am rating it a 10.  I find myself thinking about this game way too often during the day at work.  I really like the use of dice in this game - fantastic!

I also highly recommend the expansion to this game which adds in more flavor - more buildings, governor cards that give special bonuses and event cards really make no two games the same.

Update 2/15/16 :  Lowering a little.   Still enjoy it, but not as much as before.

RATING: 7.84
I enjoyed playing this game. Players roll dice, which they use to select roles and gather resources. They use these to construct buildings and fight off invasions of the Kingdom. Other than the dice mechanism which is very clever this is very similar to other worker placement games. The upside is that the game has more nuance in the turn by turn tactical play than worker placement games. The downside is that players can suffer badly from analysis paralysis and the game can slow. This is a natural product of giving players a lot of small decisions which appear to have an important future impact but which are not easily analyzed.
If you can find four or out of preference five players who will play fairly quickly - and are willing to manage random elements rather than moan about them - this is clearly going to be a fun game.

RATING: 8.4
This is a really fun game where you roll dice and then place them on a board in order to get resources. The game is simple, attractive and straightforward, with easy to learn rules and a surprising amount of strategy. There are eighteen spaces on the board, each one representing one of the various members of the kingdom (Architect, Court Jester, Smuggler, etc). Once you place your dice on a space, no one else can use that person for help. Gather resources like gold, stone and wood, build the buildings that will help your strategy the most and see what happens! Lots of fun for players young and old, both new and experienced.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
While dice are heavily used in this game, this is not your typical dice game.  Each player is governor of a realm of the kingdom, each trying to build the the better castle.  The game is divided into 5 years, with 4 seasons each year.  During three of the seasons the players are able to gain resources and build on their castle; during winter each player is attacked by a monster which can either harm the player or give the player a reward, depending on failure or success.  During each productive season all the players simultaneously roll their dice, then progressing from the lowest total to the highest each player may spend their dice to gain resources.  There are 18 advisors on the board numbered 1-18 and the dice are used to determine which advisors you can use.  For instance,f you rolled a 3-4-5 then you could use advisors 3, 4, and 5, or 7 and 5, or 3 and 9, or number 12.  Once an advisor has been claimed for that season, no other player may use them.

After gaining resources players may then spend them to build; each building offers a bonus to the player, and each player has the same choices.  The buildings are arranged in a grid on a player mat, and you have to build left to right (columns) but you can pick which rows.  Most buildings award victory points, although points are awarded for fighting off the monsters as well.

The game has a fair amount of luck involved regarding what numbers show up on the dice, however deciding what to do with your dice is a large part of the strategy behind the game.  It is light-to-medium as far as strategy is concerned, but it is far from a luck-determined game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Long, tedious, repetitive, boring. And I had such high hopes. It sounded good reading the rules, but in practice it drags even when played quickly. There's a wash, rinse, repeat cycle going for fifteen rounds and I just couldn't wait for the ride to stop.

I can see how you could get a game situation where a player falls behind and the catch-up mechanism gives them the opportunity to fight back. However, in a game with equal sensible players it really just tends to dominate. The building paths are fixed (definitely NOT Puerto Rico style), and all players seem to aim for more or less the same buildings on the same uninspiring path.

I can see how you could maybe have the occasional entertaining game due to circumstance. Personally though I think this game has some major problems that needed to be ironed out before it was released on the market. That means that I see potential - issues that could be smoothed over, It sounds like the expansion might greatly improve the game - I'd call it a "fix" and not just an expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.535
Theme: 
I like the medieval theme so that's a plus for me and whilst its not incredibly immersing it does add a nice flavour to the game.
You do get the feeling of establishing certain types of buildings and trying to sure up your defences for the oncoming monsters.

Components & Art
OK - not a lot of components in here.
There is just the board, your own dice and your personal board of buildings you can build.

Mechanics
I love dice so throwing dice is fun, and the mechanic of using your dice as effectively your action placement was something different for me to use.
It doesn't create a massive amount of luck and randomness though and whilst i am there may be 'good' strategies for making a low dice through work it feels like if you get get good dice roles early it really sets you up. 
It also feels like a game that is hard to come back from and you are reliant on the law of averages coming through - but if you start well you can mitigate your future turns by getting some good foundational buildings that cover for you
I think the expansion adds a great change with removing the dice role of the king for additional defines against monsters and gives you a little more control on your actions

Complexity
It's fairly straightforward game to learn so that's really good for getting up and going and is good filler/starter game for the night.

Final Thoughts
A good game, and happy to play it. 
The randomness/luck element can kind of dictate my feelings into the game. If you are having some bad roles can reduce the fun of it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.7
Kingsburg (English) -- Played three-, four-, and five-player.  The balancing mechanisms really only work for the ends of their respective spectrum.  For example, a tie for fewest buildings is broken by fewest goods.  So of the players tied for fewest buildings, one gains a benefit and the other gains [i]nothing[/i].  This benefit is either an additional white die before the first phase, or the King's Envoy after the Summer phase.  These are significant benefits.  I would like to see the loser of the tie receive some lesser benefit.  Similarly, the people between first and last on the turn order scale can really miss out.  (I generally avoid house rules, but the imbalance in benefits to such tied players really does seem to warrant some modification to the rules.)

That said, I've enjoyed playing this game several times, trying different strategies along the way. 

It's worth mentioning that the game is long — arguably too long for its weight.

RATING: 5.5
Played once with 3P and the expansion. I generally enjoy games where players have to work creatively with dice rolls, and the core mechanism of Kingsburg delivers in that respect. Players roll 3 dice each, and then delegate combinations of pips onto the corresponding advisors to take actions. There is some angst in figuring out how to divvy the pips, while keeping an eye out for opportunities to block opponents from coveted spots. 

However, the rest of the game is terrifically monotonous. The tech tree looks interesting at first, but it offers rather incremental abilities and the wide array of choices can inflict unwanted AP. Also, low dice rolls, like in [thing=34635][/thing], will get you nowhere. The lack of progression and repetitiveness is obviously problematic, but the nail in the coffin is that the game lasts a round too long for what it offers. It's alright, but I won't be seeking this one out to play again.

RATING: 7
Puerto Rico style buildings with special abilities, resource gathering…sure we’ve seen it all. The only difference is the use of dice to grab special abilities (Caylus style) of the King’s advisors. Each year (turn) has several similar phases and a last one where the land is attacked. Players who fail to fend off attack (usually based on value of attackers against defence) lose resource/buildings/etc. VPs are scored for buldings, etc.

The game clips along fairly nicely but is still a little long especially when everyone is trying to maximize moves towards the end game.

RATING: 7
When you want to roll some dice, this is one of the best options.  Surprisingly, the theme works for me.  Using your dice to influence the king and members of his court works well because the game effect of each persona is well integrated with their status and occupation.  Each season has an appropriate little interlude between dice rolling/influence rounds.  I especially like the battles at the end of winter.   

Only 2 things stop this from being rated higher - game length (it's a little long and difficult to teach for a light-medium weight game) and AP (this game invites players to overthink their moves which can make the game drag).  

Play with decisive cut-throat friends who like to roll dem bones and this is a "9".

RATING: 8
[2010-11-06]
Szép kivitelezés, könnyű játékmenet, de emellett jól meg kell fontolni a szerencsével kidobott kockák jó felhasználását. Nekem a játékról rögtön a Stone Age ugrott be és persze nem azért mert bármilyen erős hasonlóság lenne a játékok között, de abban mindenképp hasonlóak, hogy jó játékok, amelyek körülbelül ugyanolyan szinten támaszkodnak a kockákkal a szerencsére, mint a kőkemény taktikára. Nekem tetszenek az ilyen játékok. A kockadobások megadják a játékhangulatot és a kevésbé gondolkodós játékosoknak is tetszeni fognak. A játékban a kockadobások jó kihasználásával lehet különböző nyersanyagokat, győzelmi pontokat és haderőt gyűjteni, a nyersanyagokból fontos épületeket építeni, majd év végén legyőzni a támadó sereget a gyűjtött haderővel. A játék elég hosszú, hogy akár többféle stratégiát is ki lehessen gondolni. Kellemes játék gémereknek és nem gémereknek egyaránt.

[2011-10-08]
A második játékomban megvertek az alsó sor épületeinek gyors megépítésével működő stratégiával, amit nagyon erősnek érzetem. A harmadik játékomban kipróbáltam és nyertem vele én is. Lehet, hogy verhető, de talán csak akkor ha a többiek figyelnek erre a játékosra, illetve mások is pont ezt követik, amúgy szerintem nem tartható. Annyiban a Stone Age-nél jobb, hogy a pontszámok a végén nagyon közel vannak egymáshoz, de ha ez az egyfajta stratégia nyerő benne, akkor az nem túl jó fényt vet a játékra. Marad a pontszám még, de később eggyel csökkenhet a biztos nyerő stratégia miatt.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Action drafting with a twist.  Certainly one of the better dice games out there, well because this really isn't a dice game. The dice are more just a limiting factor to the action drafting more than they are the heart of the game.  Those that are sick of the AD mechanic should avoid this.  Yet while the focus is on action drafting, bad dice rolls will still ruin your game.  The buildings mitigate this, but you can still get worked. Still, for a big dice rolling Euro, I'd rather play Elfenwizards. 

All, and all, the game works well, and there are some significant choices that keep you engaged throughout the game.  The invading hordes aren't usually much of a threat, but because of the slight possibility that they could really mess you up, it inspires you to keep the odds in your favor, but usually the soldiers' spaces seem more a consolation prize for not getting the resource spaces you need.  

All in all there is a nice balance to the game, and it works well, but the reality of it for me is that it just doesn't merit it's length.  If the game were a third shorter I could see really wanting to get this played often.  As it, by the end of the 4th year you are really ready for the game to be over. I'd say with less players it would be quicker, but then you lose any kind of blocking on the board.  I understand why the game needs to be as long as it does (the tech tree), but because of the potential AP and the sheer number of turns it just keeps on going...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This game makes for a fantastic introduction into the world of board games. For me though, honestly, there is just too much luck. There are so many games out there that I lose all the time, but will always love to play. This game gets me very frustrated when I lose because I may have simply not gotten the good rolls. Yes, yes there are tactics that mitigate the luck, but come on! No mechanic can mitigate that much dice rolling! But, even with all the luck, I still rate it a 7 because it is well made and fun to play even though it makes me mad. Oh - and by the way, the expansion "to forge a realm" does improve the game for sure.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I am a very picky person when it comes to worker placement games.  It is probably my favorite board game style/mechanic and I have played an awful lot of them so it takes a good deal to impress me.  This one is pretty good.

Before this I had played two very similar games.  Alien Frontiers, which I didn't care for, and Troyes, which I did enjoy and actually own.  They are similar in that they are worker placement where your workers are represented by the roll of dice.  I believe the reason I didn't care for Alien Frontiers was that the actions you were able to take were mostly not based on the total of your dice roll but rather on runs and duplicates/triplicates/etc. of the results. In Kingsburg, like in Troyes, the total of your roll is all that matters.

There are some things I like in this game even more than Troyes too.  In Troyes, the higher your roll (in the end, ignoring the fact that you can have high numbered results taken from you) universally correlates to the better the action you take.  In Kingsburg this is MOSTLY true as the higher numbered rolls generally provide more stuff but sometimes you really do just need one more of a resource and only need to spend one low dice roll on it.  Also, assigning the actual values to actions rather than having actions that can be taken by any value with varying effectiveness provides a lot more opportunity for interaction and interesting competitive plays.  It is also a much simpler and therefore more accessible game than Troyes.

I will say the game does have some issues that keep me from, overall, liking it better than Troyes.  The set of 18 actions can get a little stale and lowers the shelf life of the game I think.  It's really just an increasing chart of different combinations of the games resources.  This also makes the game a bit of a luck fest because while there are opportunities, like I said, where taking a lower-numbered action is the best move the players who roll the highest numbers and consequently take the better actions are going to win 9 times out of 10.  The catch-up mechanic also doesn't really work to alleviate this problem.  After two of the rolling/building rounds the person with the fewest buildings will get either an extra die or a marker that allows you to cheat by taking an occupied action space or building two buildings in one round.  The main problem with this is that number of buildings does not universally correlate to who is weakest in the game.  In the one game I played I got these bonuses almost every round and was ahead the entire game in victory points.  The other big problem is these bonuses are huge benefits.  An extra die and not being locked out of an action space and the ability to get these while still being ahead kind of break the game.

In the end, I like the dice roll/worker placement fusion and how it is used in a lighter, more accessible game than Troyes so it is a fun game if not the most strategically interesting one.  I like the 5 different building tracks with special abilities to make the game more interesting.  And I should also mention I played just the base game and have heard almost universally that the expansion is one of those that really makes this game "complete".  I look forward to trying it again with the added content to see for myself.

I also have to add that playing this game with the full complement of 5 is the way to play.  My other game was with just 3 and there was a lot less screwage and competition over spaces.  Many might find the increase in this with more players frustrating but I consider it tense and makes the game more exciting and fun.

RATING: N/A
Copycat purchae. many plays prior to joining BGG.  introduced to us by our gaming friends, this was the next step in removing ourselves from the frustration of catan, where we had issues with long droughts of resource scarcity, kingsburg was awesome!  you get something every season!  no matter how you role, you got something...not ideal but still better than the catan robber and the *^%#%^7's you'd role.  so things we liked were the artwork, dice, the use of the dice, the fact you received something every season, other players couldn't take things from you (only blocking but thats different than stealing things from you.), finding a path of building to help acheive victory and still planning for the monsters at the end of every year.  the base game would get pretty stale so i highly recommend the expansion, to forge a realm.  that has kept it fairly fresh.  it is pretty common to get into a "building path" and play it everytime. i go wizard guild row all the time with either cathedral row for more points or the farm row.  a buddy will use the mint row (with expansion) to great success.  another will always try for embasssy row.   once we have had various levels of success with our strategies, we don't really branch away from it.  so it just comes down to who roles best and can get the most from their dice.  but there in lies the fun of dice rolling....

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is an interesting “worker placement” game where instead of workers you have influence in the form of dice that are rolled at the beginning of the turn. These three dice can be split up and summed and placed on spots on the board that give some sort good or bonus to the player.  Each spot can only be influenced by one player so there is jockeying for position for the juicier spots on the board.  The goods earned are used to purchase buildings which earn the player bonuses and victory points.  In addition, the buildings are in columns and rows, you need to purchase a level one building before building a level two building, and so on, up to the best, which is level four.  The die rolling mechanic is pretty cool because it forces you to change you plans when you roll poorly.  I am not a fan of the battle mechanic, rolling a die and adding bonuses to determine if you beat the baddies, is too much random proven by the fact that it was changed in the expansion.  I am also wondering if this game is too luck based or if good play can overcome the dice.  To accomplish this, I would like to track the overall sum of dice from each player and see if it correlates to player finish.

RATING: 6.5
[3 plays] My take was not a bad game, not a great game.  In general I like resource games like this.  They are among my favorite types.  I am less fond of rolling to dice to see what resources I get, although in this one because you roll three dice and can combine them in so many different ways it actually worked pretty well.  My biggest dislike was rolling the dice to see if we defeated the monster at the end of every year.  The difference between rolling a 1 vs a 6 is huge, is there is a lot of difference between getting rewarded for defeating the monster compared to getting a penalty for losing.  I would not mind playing this game a few more times to get a better feel for it.  Try some different strategies for what resources to go after, which building row to focus on etc.  It seems like it could be a fun game to learn better and try different things.  However, after this play I didn't find it anywhere near as enjoyable as something like Caylus where I don't have to roll dice to place my workers, and there is no monster that I randomly could either win or lose too.  NOTE: the expansion fixes my biggest issue with dice rolling for the monster fights.  Really the expansion is a must for this game.

RATING: N/A
Il reame di Kingsburg è sotto attacco! Mostruosi eserciti invasori sono alle porte e cercano di impossessarsi del regno. Sei stato nominato governatore di una provincia di confi­ne per amministrarla e difenderla. Kingsburg e’ un gioco di strategia in cui i dadi sono le tue risorse! A seconda di come deciderai di usarli, potrai ottenere oro, soldati o materiali da costruzione. Riuscirai a rendere la tua provincia la piu’ ricca del re­gno e allo stesso tempo a difenderla dai pericoli che la minacciano? Scegli la tua strategia: me­glio puntare sulla forza militare o sull’economia? Cosa costruire, la nuova cattedrale o la gilda dei maghi? A te la scelta, ma solo il migliore entrerà nelle grazie di re Tritus!

Elenco elementi di gioco
1 Tabellone principale
5 Tabelloni provincia
21 dadi a sei facce
17 segnalini colorati
1 segnalino "agente del Re"
16 gettoni "+2"
60 cubetti di legno
85 gettoni edificio
25 carte invasori
1 regolamento

RATING: 8
Something of an oddity - an economic engine / development game disguised as a fantasy dice-fest. Players compete Caylus-like for sole right to choose particular actions, but dice rolls dictate your choices. Add together your dice and go for a single big/powerful action, or split the dice and take a number of less powerful actions? Your choice. Looking forward to future plays, especially with more players.

RATING: 2.5

If you are a person who enjoys exceedingly long times of dice chucking then Kingsburg is your game. I just hoped that it was over sooner rather than later.

RATING: 7
26-Sep-08: Played this with the Java AI. I thought that it had mild interaction (ie. if you can take something before someone else can, do so), but that's about it. Fairly straightforward; less of a game and more of an activity sort of thing. Pick a strategy and stick to it, swear at the dice rolls, etc.

11-Oct-10: Played Kingsburg with Cynthia on Sun arvo. Not too bad with 2p, but i think it's better with 3p+. There's not enough ability to hinder the leader with 2p. I can't see how Kingsburg can be played with 5p in 90 mins though (as stated on box).

27-Oct-10: I feel that it's a good game but just suffers from beng a wee bit too long. I feel that the Market is almost too crucial though. The joy of screwage/risk taking comes from trying to predict what dice other people are going to use and then taking the action before they do. The Market waters this aspect down. I've heard that the Queen was originally designed to give only 2 VPs - the designers calculated that her VP worth is really 2.5 VPs and rounded up instead of down, but now most feel that they should have rounded down instead. 3 VPs makes the Queen too good to pass up. No one really takes the King, which is a pity since you need that rare 3 sixes roll. I'd love to give the game another go, but this time with the Battle Tokens (either self-made or bought).

RATING: 10
Boardgame warehouse

Really fun worker/dice placement game. 3 dice determine you options for resource collection and army building.

Bottom row of buildings seems to always be a strong choice. I have played it using a variety and I usually do well. My wife always seems to be buying points because of that bottom row and seems to win a lot.

Expansion is almost a must. Game could get pretty stale without it.

Easy to teach and fun to play. I can't decide if the 18-King or the 17-Queen is a better option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5

When I first played Kingsburg, it was like playing a deckbuilder for the first time - it blew me away with its unique yet fun system, I guess you would call it dice placement? The idea of dice in a euro game had always seemed like it wouldn't work, and this was one of the first games that made me realise, it can. The dice don't dictate what you have to do, but only limit your options, sometimes meaning you have to make some tough choices. 

After a few plays it does get a bit repetitive, and my suspicion is that the expansion will greatly remedy this. But Kingsburg has definitely got me interested in trying more dice placement games.

RATING: 7
Man, I hate to keep giving games a "7" rating, but Kingsburg just fits that criteria so well.  It is just on the cusp of getting a higher rating, but I remember how I felt the last time I owned it....

The story is, we had it, played the heck out of it, and then I felt both Caron and I had kind of reached a plateau with it.  I felt that we had kind of gotten to this point where the game kind of went the same way every time--not a good sign.  So I traded it.

Big mistake.  After I traded it, Caron was really not cool with me trading it.  Seems that she really did like it--quite a bit.  So, after trading it almost two years ago, I got it back.

Well, what about the game?  It is nothing spectacular whatsoever.  It is a typical Euro game about influencing advisors, gaining favor, blah blah blah.  But, dice are stuck into the game as the fuel for the engine, making for large amounts of chance.  We just played a game that was so close, I had one chance to win, and that came on the roll of a die.  So, the game, even though it has a great amount of strategy in it, can really hinge on the roll of a single die.  If that kills a game for you, then stay away.  

But, if you don't mind a lighter weight game that allows for strategy, conversation, and the occasional head scratching AP moments, then give Kingsburg a spin.  It is a good game for what it is.  Nothing earth shattering, but I feel it is a pretty enjoyable ride.

RATING: 7.3
Kingsburg is a bit unusual for a Euro, it feels at first like an Ameritrash game because you are rolling a fistful of dice every turn.  But from there it goes in a very different direction.  The dice are used to pick roles (similar in nature but not in function to Puerto Rico).  And the roles help you construct buildings.  The battle at the end of each year can be brutal, but overall Kingsburg is an interesting game that meshes fast play with some deep strategic thinking at times.  There are faster and deeper games out there, but none quite like this hybrid.  If you are in to trying something different, give Kingsburg a whirl.

RATING: 8.5
Kingsburg is a worker placement game with a twist in that rolled dice represent the workers. I'm typically no fan at all of dice games, but the way the mechanic is utilized in Kingsburg does a good job at minimizing the random element sufficiently that it is rare a good player cannot work around the rolls. The tiered building mechanic is well designed and makes this game accessible to a broad audience. My only criticism of this game is that it lacks long term replayability. The expansion helped add some flavor but not enough so to put the excitement back in.

RATING: 6
One of the games that belonged in my Attia Family Tree article, but which I hadn't had the opportunity to try beforehand (fortunately the BoardGameGeek community helped fill in the gaps and wrote an explanation of why Kingsburg belonged in the conversation, which I included in my follow-up Grandfather of Worker Placement article. Now, a little over a year later, I've finally tried Kingsburg and confirmed its placement in the family tree. Of all the twists on worker placement that each game has brought to the table, I have to say that Kingsburg's is certainly one of the most innovative. Substituting dice for each player's workers is not somewhere I could honestly have predicted the family going, but hindsight is 20/20, so after the fact it seems like a natural innovation (a contradiction I suppose). Unlike Caylus with its growing expanse of potential locations to place your workers, Kingsburg presents you at the outset with 18 locations numbered 1 through 18. You roll three dice and take turns assigning them to the locations that match the sum of the die or dice assigned to that location. Thus, reaching the coveted 18 location requires a roll of three sixes (or the necessary dice modification tools procured through other game locations). That's essentially where the innovation stops though because like just about every worker placement game, the locations give players some combination of wood, stone, gold, and other such resources, which players convert into buildings worth victory points and special abilities. It's certainly a clever game and one I'd happily play again, but something ethereal kept it from quite reaching the threshold of purchasing the game. It's always hard to pin down exactly what makes a game worthy of being added to your collection versus being relegated to only playing someone else's copy, but whatever spark is necessary was missing for me despite all of the game's tangibly good qualities.

RATING: 6
What initially intrigued me about this game was the dice-as-workers worker placement mechanic used when influencing the advisors on the board.  Although this mechanic is slightly interesting, I was underwhelmed by its implementation here.  It does create interesting decisions when trying to decide which advisors you need to influence before your opponents get them, but that's about all this game has.  I don't like the way a game round has to go through eight different phases, each slightly different.  Maybe it provides balance, but it feels fiddly and unnecessarily complicated.  I really don't like the "combat system" where the players have to fight invaders each round.  Way too much is on the line for one die roll.  The end of the game doesn't feel tense at all because pretty much all of the points have already been distributed, the winner is almost always completely predictable.  I would play it again but I don't really feel the need to.

RATING: 8
Kingsburg bring the fun of rolling dice back to real board game play. There are just enough ways to manipulate the dice to keep them fair while still keeping them random. A nice building track with interesting buildings allow for multiple strategies as well. My biggest complaint about Kingsburg is the "Kings Reinforcement Dice" rolled before you battle in the winter. While I understand why it's there, it adds a random element that you can't really effect or predict. In a game that's already walking the fine like of randomness, this single die roll pokes it ever so slightly over the line.

Even with that said, I greatly enjoyed the game and can't wait until our next play to try something new.
