=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The game takes place over five years, a total of 20 turns. In every year, there are 3 production seasons for collecting resources, building structures, and training troops. Every fourth turn is the winter, in which all the players must fight an invading army. Each player must face the invaders, so this is not a cooperative game.
The resources to build structures and train troops are collected by influencing the advisers in the King's Council. Players place their influence dice on members of the Council. The player with the lowest influence dice sum will be the first one to choose where to spend his/her influence; this acts as a way of balancing poor dice rolling. Even with a very unlucky roll, a clever player can still come out from the Council with a good number of resources and/or soldiers.
Each adviser on the King's Council will award different resources or allocate soldiers, victory points, and other advantages to the player who was able to influence him/her for the current turn.
At the end of five years, the player who best developed his assigned territory and most pleased the King through the Council is the winner.
Many alternate strategies are possible to win: will you go for the military way, disregarding economic and prestige buildings, or will you aim to complete the big Cathedral to please the King? Will you use the Merchant's Guild to gain more influence in the Council, or will you go for balanced development?

RATING: 7
Ingenious game system, with good 2 player rules. I love dice rolling games and this has a lot of good features from games like Caylus, Puerto Rico, and Outpost. On the down side, players who take forever thinking about possibilities can have a field day here. Also, it is very difficult to come from behind and pull off a win, in spite of the built in catch up features. Yet I keep playing, hoping to find a way!

RATING: 7.5
With five players, the game got quite long, so I prefer to play it with 3 of 4 players. I like that the game is so simple to explain and understand, that all information is "out in the open" (no drawing of cards, no differences in abilities or powers) and the game's components and artwork are attractive and very clear. In the end it is a light, but engrossing game in which luck plays a remarkably small role considering the dice: The winner was not the player who rolled best. Also, in both cases, the winner was not clear until the last round, making those last turns very suspenseful indeed. Looking forward to playing this again (and I am not the only one)!

RATING: 8
The bastard offspring of Puerto Rico, In The Year of the Dragon and Can't Stop. Five years - end of every year the bad guys are going to come a calling and wreck something. You have three seasons to meet with advisors, get stuff from them to erect buildings and recruit soldiers so by the time winter hits and the bad guys show up you can chase them away with your trooops and gain favour from the King. One problem - the dice decide which advisors you can call on and most of the time they can only meet one person per season, so tough luck if you don't get to the one you need first. There's some AP with allocating the dice but that speeds up as the players get used to the game. I really liked it after one play, even though there's limited player interaction (which I would say is its only real flaw.)

RATING: 5.5
Take the worker placement mechanic from Caylus / Leonardo / AoEIII, but randomize your workers each turn so each player will have differently abled workers to grab powers: welcome to Kingsburg. While I love worker placement games, and will play Caylus or Leonardo Da Vinci anytime, anyplace, the random worker thing took a lot of the fun out of the mechanic for me. There isn't as much anguish if only 2 players can vie for special power x because of the pip count everyone has, and there is no anguish if your dice roll gives you a lock on something nice. Unlike Caylus, there aren't even painful choices over controling turn order-- here it is simply a matter of high rolling. An extra smidgen of tedium came from the lack of differentiation in the building tracks. After all, with only 2 soldier spots on the board, and the need for soldiers ever escalating, everyone will have to basically get quite a few   defense buildings. That makes a lot of the building choices obvious. All in all it had neither the tension that I crave in games nor enough brutal decisions. Did I mention it feels long for what it is? On the positive side, it sure feels nice to roll crazy high numbers all night. I should have been at a craps table with the luck I had!

RATING: 8
Alright, I've only played Kingsburg once, so I don't have a great idea of the involved strategy. This will be more first impressions, so I might need to adjust my score accordingly after more plays. 

After first play I really liked Kingsburg. It is a very basic worker placement game where you you can build up certain tracks/specialties. This allows a couple different paths to victory which is nice for replayability. My biggest gripe with this is that every player as the exact same track, however I hear the expansion fixes this. There is also kind of a cool mechanic where all the players need to work together to fight off an attack and the end of a round. 

Overall, I liked this game on first try. Components were nice as was artwork. A good entry level worker placement euro game. I hope to play it more though.

RATING: 7
Great, innovative use of dice, similar to [gameid=22345] in that manner.  Beautiful components, and enjoyable (though generic) theme.  I have not played many games of this, but I am interested to know whether it would hold up past plays 5-10.  There's certainly lots of variability in how you roll your dice and how the other players choose to distribute theirs, but building patterns tend to cluster.  Some building paths are popular, and it can result in people taking very similar strategies game after game.  (To be fair, the expansion addresses this.)  This is definitely worth a play, and might be worth buying if your collection needs some quick games for newer gamers or lighter gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I am a big fan of worker placement, and initially I really really liked the game because of its use of dice as workers and how easy it is to teach.  However, my enthusiasm for the game waned as time went on.  I realized that the game is easy to teach because it is really quite shallow.  There are multiple ways to go about adding to your tableau (which is supposed to add variety to forging a strategy), but it seems as though there's really only one way to win.  Since there is really only one way to win, whoever gets to it first tends to win.  To me, that is defeating the purpose of worker placement mechanics and why I wouldn't rank this game any higher than a 6.

RATING: 7
I have played many a game of Kingsburg and have yet to turn one down. A worker placement/engine-building/dicey box of fun. I love that you are simultaneously trying to outguess your opponents and leave the most options open for yourself. After MANY plays this one can feel a little samey. However, if you are playing with equally skilled opponents it is a very tight and enjoyable game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Играл полпартии. На мой взгляд ничего сильного в игре нет.

1. Множество разбросанных компонентов, и еще с мелкими значками. Например 17 советников, каждый дает что-то свое. 20 зданий, каждое чем-то отличается, причем при строительстве оно на общем планшете отмечается фишкой, и у каждого игрока свой планшет. В итоге сложно ориентироваться или определять какие бонусы у кого есть, или какие могут быть.

2. Броски кубиков каждый сезон. Причем они не просто не дают следовать какой-то нужной стратегии, но еще и чем меньше выбросишь, тем реально хуже.

3. Набеги в конце года как-то не впечатлили. Ну ладно они случайной силы и эффекта, так еще и мы бросаем кубик. Ну ладно еще что сил может не хватить случайно, так еще и обидно, когда старался, наращивал силу, а тут кубик справился с набегом сам по себе - зачем старался...

4. Разнообразие зданий и набегов как-то тоже не впечатляет. Ну например механика битв ну совершенно неинтересна (а я считаю что в игре все должно быть интересно). Ну и здания которые отличаются типа как "я даю +1 в битве", а "я +2 и еще 1 ПО, но дороже" - не цепляет. Скажем 8-й ролей в Цитаделях значительно разнообразней чем 20 построек в Кингсбурге.

5. Ну и конечно игра просто на очки и без нормального взаимодействия (отбирать советников у других - едва ли здоровый пример взаимодействия).

На мой взгляд игра бы сильно выиграла, если бы планшет был один на всех игроков (и два игрока не могут построить одинаковое здание). Ну а бросок кубика перед сражением лучше вообще убрать.

RATING: 6
This is a neat take on the dice-rolling genre.  There are enough options here to make it more than JUST a dice-rolling game, much in the same way that Yspahan does the same thing.  I see a lot of different influences from other games, but it puts a little more fun into those formulas, and makes for a great game to play with family or children.  I recommend playing the Java implementation of the game just to learn the mechanics, and to get an idea of how the game flows.

After several plays against the computer, I wonder if the same sort of strategy is optimal for each game.  Of course, this doesn't mean I win against the computer, but it does make me wonder if people tend to follow the same strategy each time they play the game.

50+ plays: computer
3 plays: 4 players
4 plays: 5 players

RATING: N/A
In Kingsburg, players are Lords sent from the King to administer frontier territories.
\13\\13\
The game takes place over five years, a total of 20 turns. In every year, there are 3 production seasons for collecting resources, building structures, and training troops. Every fourth turn is the winter, in which all the players must fight an invading army. Each player must face the invaders, so this is not a cooperative game. The resources to build structures and train troops are collected by influencing the advisers in the King's Council. Players place their influence dice on members of the Council. The player with the lowest influence dice sum will be the first one to choose where to spend his/her influence; this acts as a way of balancing poor dice rolling. Even with a very unlucky roll, a clever player can still come out from the Council with a good number of resources and/or soldiers. Each adviser on the King's Council will award different resources or allocate soldiers, victory points, and other advantages to the player who was able to influence him/her for the current turn. At the end of five years, the player who best developed his assigned territory and most pleased the King through the Council is the winner.
\13\\13\
Note: Kingsburg: To Forge a Realm expansion is stored with the base game

RATING: 7
A worker placement game that makes clever use of dice for claiming resources and scoring VPs. There is more luck then usual for a game of this type, but I think it makes things a bit more exciting than the usual perfect information Euro. The randomness also serves to drive the game's building aspect, as quite a few of the building spaces allow a player to manipulate future dice rolls. This small bit of control isn't perfect...If a player rolls consistently high, they will probably win, but it usually takes a particularly long hot or cold streak to really imbalance things.

Overall, I'd say Kingsburg was a neat, but slightly repetitive game. I highly recommend the expansion, "To Forge a Realm". It addresses a few issues with the game, adds a mountain of re-playability and (in my book, anyway) about a point and a half to the rating!

RATING: 6.5
A fun worker-placement game that has enough randomness to keep it challenging but not overly frustrating. I personally think it plays best with 3 and 4, as 5-players creates just a bit too much tension over the available spots on the board and said dice rolls can really impact your options. But with fewer players, it never becomes too much of a problem.

If I have a complaint, it's that the game does tend to run a little long and that there often isn't much of a surprise in the endgame scoring. Most often, the lead player will be apparent by the last few turns of the game, unless set upon by some very unfortunate events.

Still, a fun game and good as a "gateway" for non-gamers.

I've never played Caylus, but it seems similar in that the turn order changes, and is represented by coloured wood tokens.  Also you "earn favour", build, and there is a scoring track around the outside of the board.

Also to Dominion in that advisors you earn favour from all give you different bonuses similar to playing an action card in Dominion. 

Want to play again and might purchase.

RATING: 7
Played a few times face to face (with 3 to 5 players) and on BSW (with 2 or 3 players). An interesting game with a clever use of dice. What matters most is not to roll high dice, but to be able to make good use of all your dice (blocking can definitely happen with 5 players - it is less of a problem with fewer, which in turn increates the influence of luck). 
It looks like there are several possible strategies, depending on the buildings you want to specialize in - but it seems difficult to do without the (cheap) buildings in the first two columns, and I am not sure how much variety it really gives in the end. 
Also, the abundance of different tie-breakers in various circumstances is a bit annoying. But I think the main possible problem which will prevent me from liking this game more is that it feels a bit long for what it is. Still, I am looking forward to the expansion.

RATING: 7
Played at Essen 2007. Kingsburg involves a dice rolling mechanic to decide which goods you get, and therefore which buildings you can build. The higher you roll the better, but there's usually something worthwhile you can do with your dice no matter what you roll. There are also a couple of reasonable catch-up mechanisms to help those who roll abysmally. 

There are a few paths you can explore depending on which buildings you go for, there's some tension over whether other players will grab the best spots before you, and whether you'll be able to defeat the enemies at the end of each year.

The conversion of goods cubes into beneficial abilities and progress along various bonus tracks is nothing new, but it works well enough here.

RATING: 7

This game would have dropped to a 6/6.5 over time just from the feeling that the paths are obvious and fairly balanced. There is not too much luck just your choices do not seem strategically important enough to outweigh it. Why did it not drop? Because the expansion brings both more choices and more interesting events that change up each game. The use of tokens for troops at the end of the year adds a nice bump in strategy also.

RATING: 7
My wife and I really enjoy Kingsburg.  I was surprised at how much she enjoys the game.  It is one of her favorites.  I enjoy how you can manipulate the dice and the vast technology track.  The tech. track provides a lot of variety in strategies to pursue from game to game.  One of my favorite parts of this game is the risk vs. reward aspect.  Do you not peek at the monster but risk losing the fight later on because you don't have a strong enough defense in order to take another bonus?  The artwork is top notch and really brings you into the game.  I have played this with kids and they enjoy it a lot as well. The fantasy theme really draws them in.  I'd call this a small step up from a gateway game. 

Note:  Sleeved with Mayday Standard USA Premium Sleeves (purple pack)

RATING: 8
I am not as taken by this game as the similar [gameid=34635].  It looks like there are only a couple of ways to get points - the embassy, the fortress (winning battles) and the farm and [i]hope[/i] you roll the queen often.  At this time I am more willing and wanting to play Stone Age while my wife is more willing to play this one.

After more plays, there are some interesting things going on with this and I am a little more enchanted by it.  I am looking forward to [gameid=39775].  I am really looking forward to the two rows of extra buildings and character cards.  I think these will address at least one of the concerns I have with this game - there is only one or two winning strategies.  As I play more, this one does have some staying power.

Plays after July 25, 2009 include [gameid=39775]

RATING: 9
I like the dice rolling and commitment combination. What originally felt a bit hampered -- since there didn't appear to be that many dice rolls to help smooth out luck over the course of a game -- isn't actually that big a deal, as there are several nice, subtle catch up mechanics and the game has a very brisk pace. Some of the special abilities of the buildings help soften the impact. And some nice planning out to block opponents' moves based on die combinations. Some players may experience a very slight slowdown by AP as more dice/number combos lead to more choice evaluations for a 5-player game, but it's minimal. Excellent components. A great 3 player game. [Update] After running exactly 50 demos of Kingsburg at GenCon -- I STILL love the darn game. That says an awful lot!

RATING: 6
As feared the repetitive dice rolling for 3 seasons each year for a total of 15 times was more than I would have cared for.  The rest of the game felt like an advanced version of To Court the King.  Maybe that's not a totally fair assessment, as there is actually a bit more consideration required when choosing the kind of resources you want to obtain, as well as which character to influence so you can utilize all of your dice.  However, more or less it came down to the luck of the roll, then you just mix and match to come up with the best combination you can get.  The wintery raid seem rather superfluous, since rarely does one actually succumb to the disaster, so the inclusion of such element felt more like a nuisance rather than essential to the gameplay.

The production for the most part is wonderful, the mechanics just failed to interest me.  Won't likely play it again any time soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a pretty cool game with way way WAY too much downtime and length for what it provides the player.  I think I would rate this an [b][bgcolor=#66ff99]&nbsp;8&nbsp;[/bgcolor][/b] if it were a computer game you played against AIs.  One other bother about the game is that high rolls are strictly better.  This is a bothersome trait for dice games, because your odds of rolling high are equal to your odds of rolling low.  Let's just flip a coin, and declare that heads is always the better flip while we're at it.  To Court the King (Um Krone und Kragen) mitigates this much better, in that straights and n-of-a-kind are the primary beneficial patterns, and by the time you really need to deal with high rolls being strictly better, you have had plenty of opportunities to get abilities that can increase your die rolls.  On the other hand, Kingsburg provides a little bit of a plot, as you progress and build, hoping to fend off increasingly difficult foes, whose strength is only vaguely known, unless you sacrifice opportunities to see what the creatures are.

Seriously, I would love to play this against only AIs.

RATING: 5
After 5+ Plays: I really liked this one after trying it for the first time, but subsequent plays all unfolded in essentially the same way.  Without variance in the buildings, it is too easy to follow the same strategy every game.  In addition, I think the push-your-luck element of the battle is out of place and poorly implemented, as there are too few dice rolls to make it even out over the course of the game.  It seems that the expansion will address both of these issues, so I look forward to trying it.

After 10+ plays: I really like the expansion, and will not play without it now.

RATING: 7
The expansions seem almost like a requirement to me. Otherwise there are certain aspects of the base game that turn me off, like the random number of soldiers you get at the end of each year, and the similarities in pursued strategies without the character cards.

Also, the fact that constantly rolling very low will still make it harder for you to perform well is kind of a bummer (as opposed to, say, Troyes, where you can use pretty much any number, but for different purposes, and you can buy other players' dice as well).
